RATING: 8
I really enjoy playing this game. It is very colorful, I love the veggie meeples, and there is enough helper cards to incite player interaction and prevent it from just being multiplayer solitaire.

That being said, the down time is pretty incredible, especially in a 3p game (avoid this at all costs). If Loyang was a 60 minute game I would probably bump the rating to an 8, but for the two of us it is a 2 hour game at minimum, and almost double that for a 3p game. By comparison, we can quickly run through a 2p Agricola game in under an hour. I'm not interested in an Arkham-length Uwe Rosenberg brain burner. At that length, I would rather play another game from our collection like Mage Knight. Hopefully the game length improves with more game plays.

UPDATED 10-10-14: Played it again recently. I like it a lot but only as a 2 players game for now. I'd like to give it a try with 4 players using the 2-at-a-time rule so it plays like a 2 players game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Economic game of short term gain vs. long term investment, with a farming theme.

The goal of the game is to score the most points by the end of nine set rounds, of which each round consists of 4 phases.  In phase one, you plough a new field and simultaneously harvest your already growing vegetables.  Phase two is a fairly unique card-drafting system, where you start with 4 cards each, and on your turn you can either discard a card into the common pool (with the possibility of later obtaining that card) or decide to keep ONE card in your hand and ONE card from the common pool.  So if you start with a crap hand or a great hand, well you're only getting (probably) one of those cards, although with some planning and luck, you could get one more of them from the common pool.  Phase 3 is the action phase, which is done solitaire style- you have several different actions to choose from, which can be done in any order.  Actions include: buying a two-pack of cards, which gives you more action flexibility, selling vegetables for money to your shop (a weak but sometimes necessary action), buying vegetables from your shop (expensive and therefore weak but sometimes necessary action), selling vegetables to either regular and/or casual customers (a strong money-making move, but not without some tradeoffs and timing issues), sowing vegetables you currently possess for future investments, using helper cards (which really are generally pretty helpful), and using market trading cards.  Phase 4 consists of buying points (using money), and it involves an interesting mechanism where the points are initially very cost-effective (when you of course don't have money to spare), but get alot more expensive as the game progresses.  

Well Uwe does it again, sort of.  I think this is the weakest of his big three, which is to say it's a really good game, rather than a great game.  My wife really likes this game as she does all of the other big three, so this game is getting alot of play time, and with more plays, strategy is becoming a bit more clear, although the card-draw randomness can be pretty significant.  Gameplay feels light, and I'd say this is the lighter than Agricola and Le Havre, although it's still a "gamer's game".  It's really a 2-player game that was converted into a 2-4 (1-4) player game; 2 players is great, 4 is ok, and 3-player, while still pretty good, has some real downtime (more than a 4-player oddy enough).  Components are good, very professionally produced for a new company especially, but suprisingly few components for the cost (I know economies of scale, and the whole bit, but still...).  This is really begging for a mass release, as it's a very accessible and interesting game- Z-Man to the rescue for the masses.

RATING: 8
Initial rating. Will refine with subsequent plays.

UPDATE: This is a brain-burner, for certain. Personally, I frequently find action combinations that solve a problem I have in the game, but only after carefully thinking things through. Whenever I get "stuck" I keep thinking that there's a possibility to reason it out and get "unstuck." This kind of observation leads me to believe that analysis paralysis could develop... hence I would suggest a timer (loosely applied, in this case) for the action phase to speed things along.

This is not to say this isn't a FANTASTIC GAME... it really is. It's just that there seems to be creative ways to solve problems in which you find yourself -- something I really like about this.

At the Gates of Loyang is different enough from Agricola, but similar enough as well. While this IS a fantastic game, I don't believe that it compares to the heavier Agricola/Le Havre pair of games. It's easy to see how THIS game inspired Agricola (not the other way around, judging from release dates). Not a bad title by any means. I simply PREFER Agricola and Le Havre to this, that's all.

RATING: 6
It's another sprawling affair - this time with cards that take up alot of space. The rules system is interesting, but a touch unrefined. The card distribution rules are especially odd and overly done for such a simple effect. Also, it's excruciating to get money early on which dampens fun - until you understand the way of trade with vegetables and merchants . Later, money is rolling - which also dampens fun. The best moments are the middle game when you start to turn the corner from difficulty to smooth sailing. So all in all, a mixed bag for me. Additional plays are continuing to confirm and in fact lower my impression. Might end up on the trade pile.

RATING: 5
Take Jambo, make the cards vastly less interesting, bring in the Antiquity mechanism, and make it repetitive.  Make it repetitive.  Make it repetitive.

The last bit isn't quite true -- the beginning is tense (you have no money and no stuff, and need to get it quick) and the middle is not bad (you have stuff and can make plans and money effectively), but there the arc ends:  there is no real "endgame" (other than a point of little return on field planting), just an extended middle.  That goes on a little too long.  Your business gets "good" but not "great."  Compare to Jambo, where the replacement of a fixed timer with the 60 gold Rubicon creates strong endgame tension and analysis of how to prepare a strong finishing hand, and whether you are well placed to end the game.

I think some optimization can be done and the luck isn't complete, but it isn't very exciting.  It's not terrible, just not that great.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
1-4(2)

Really, it's mostly an efficiency exercise in figuring out how to accumulate markets and customers and helpers and fulfill them in a maximal way, sort of like the Traveling Salesman Problem.

It plays this way, too. It's VERY solitary ... the helpers are only occasionally (and weakly) interactive, and the drafting has elements of this, but it's also weak. There's also a whole lot of bit-pushing, and a lot of it is just obligatory (as in, not requiring thought, just "doing what needs to be done"). There's also a whole lot of mental bookkeeping: remembering if you've bought a two-pack, if you've delivered to a regular (or not), if you've paid for your "free" point already, etc. All of which I think make what could be an interesting puzzle into something of a chore.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6

RATING: 7
A wonderful mid-weight euro that is accessible, puzzly, and still manages to feel refreshing/unique years after its initial release. The game has great tension, interesting decisions, and an outstanding production quality. And while there is significant randomness, it is somewhat controlled (drafting), appropriate for the weight/length of the game, and seems to add more to the depth of the game than reduce any players' decisions. 

Unfortunately, the helpers in the game end up being a double-edge sword. They add depth and makes the game more interactive. But they also create a situation where players must wait for another player to complete his/her turn. And often, players won't play their helper(s), which is why the other player(s) waited. Thus, the downplay is noticeable and somewhat more annoying as it is often in vain/unnecessary/etc.

RATING: 7
Nekem kicsit csalódás az Agricola után. Szép ez is, játszom vele szívesen, de valahogy más kategória így elsőre. 

A játékélménytől függetlenül pedig: nagyon kevés az anyag a dobozban, főleg az árához képest. Mondhatni: túl drága:)

Van egy kiskapu a játékban (talán hibának is nevezhetnénk), amivel plusz 2 Cash-t szerezhetünk az elején (Kölcsönt veszünk fel, és lépünk egyet előre 3 pénzért). Ezt akár oda is adhatnák előre.
---
Na másodjára már sokkal jobb volt!
---
Újra előkerült: nem rossz játék ez, sokkal egyedibb, mint pl. az Agricola, DE nagyon monoton, feleslegesen sok a 9 kör. Ahogy egyik játékostársam mondta: nincs íve. Ugyanez az élmény lenne 5 körben is, csak akkor nem lenne az az érzésem, hogy "de hosszú volt...".
---
Nem rossz, nem rossz, de az Agricolában vagy Le Havre-ban sokkal több újrajátszási lehetőséget érzek. Persze amíg évente egyszer kerül elő, addig mindegy:)
Egyébként Oregonnal egy napon játszottuk, és az Oregon dobozába belefért a Loyang is, így nem kellett két dobozt vinnem... Azért az Agricolát nem nagyon lehetne belepréselni csak úgy mellékesen egy másik dobozba:) Hiába a sok zöldség, valahogy az az érzésem, hogy nincs benne anyag. Ennek ellenére horror áron volt.

RATING: 5
Loyang feels much more like a cardgame than Agricola or Le Havre.  It definitely lacks the long term planning and sheer variety that the other two games in the Harvest Trilogy offer.  Basically, Loyang is sort of a step in the wrong direction.  Why should I spend a lot of time playing Loyang when the later offerings are far superior?  I guess the answer is that Loyang is worth a couple of plays.  I do enjoy the harvesting and planting.  Trying to figure out how to satisfy your regular customers is an interesting puzzle.  The two pack also adds some excitement and fun, but it also increases the luck of the card draw. 

The main downside to Loyang is that it really feels like multi-player solitaire.  The Helpers add a touch of interaction, but they also provide a screwage factor that is hard to control.  To be honest, Loyang would probably be better with less interaction.  It certainly would cut down on the downtime.  Anyway, I'd like to entirely replace the interactive helpers with new helpers that don't screw people.   

Update: It turns out that Loyang is best as a solo game.

RATING: 9
Lok: BHKJ010401
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Initial experience:

There are some clever mechanisms here that are enjoyable to work with.  But there is so much randomness that it doesn't really matter in the end.  Tight games are going to come down to who got the perfect card in their hand for their situation, and the player on their left throwing away a fantastic card for them.  If they can do that with a good turn-order, they're up for the round.

The common pool/turn order sounds like it would work on the surface, but once your inside you see how it doesn't work as smoothly as one would hope.  Either all players should play to their last card, or to their second card if there's something particularly juicy for their position.  Trouble is, even in a 4-player game, the players all need different things, so it's rarely a conflict when someone takes the card that is best suited to them.  In addition, players are throwing away their most worthless cards early on in the phase.  So the whole turn-order mechanism makes no sense.

The scores are so tight here, it's somewhat reminiscent of Power Grid.  Except in Power Grid, money being the tie-breaker is usually what wins the game - money is King, and based on the design, it makes sense.  Here, money is a by-product/end-of-the-refinement-process, and it feels a bit off to have the game be 19.5 -19.7-19.10, 5 coins made the difference between first and third.  This is the primary effect of the scoring mechanism, which doesn't so much stop runaway leaders, as guarantee that all players are going to be extremely close to one another in points.

The sowing/harvesting is great, an idea that has been around for a while in Rosenberg's games.  I had a great "credit crunch" game in which I owed regular customers more than I could handle, and needed to keep producing more and more, but when I grabbed two-fers, I got *more* regular customers, so I owed *more* goods.

That said, the randomness of those two-fers is *ridiculous* and simply breaks the game of anything interesting it may otherwise exist in this game.

So, while the sowing, harvesting, grocers, and customer mechanics are all good, they are all tied together by far too much randomness to be worth any while.  The good news is that the randomness is mostly resolved before your main action turn, so you can mostly plan around it.  But sometimes, randomness is a major player in the game - and I don't like playing with that player.

Better than Le Havre, not as good as Agricola.

11/24/09:

After two 4-player games, I'm done with it.  I might play it solo if I ever get the chance, but I will be actively avoiding instances of the game in the future.  When a game is forced to play this tightly (by scores), the randomness determines far too much.

I'd rather play Le Havre, I think.  While it's an uninteresting game, at least randomness doesn't play such a key aspect.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
I never play eurogames gladly, as they are all about their precious rulesystems and don’t even care for theme, story, randomness, freedom and real interactions among the players: in other words, they’re too cold and highbrow for my tastes.

The typical example of such annoying games is Agricola, a deeply unthematic multiplayer solitaire so fiddly, dry and complex that I usually lose any interest in playing it (let alone winning) way before the first turn ends: so I wasn’t thrilled at all when I’ve been first invited to play At the Gates of Loyang, which comes from the same author as Agricola’s and openly tries to capitalize on the Arcadian vein opened by its more renowned and successful forerunner.

Yet surprisingly I found in Loyang one of those very few euros I can moderately stand, as long as I have to play them only once in a very long while: like its likes indeed, Loyang is opposed to fun and mirth but at least unlike those it offers a somewhat more thematic experience and looks a bit less abstract than most of the euros (and Agricola ahead of them all).

Of course I’m never a hopeful challenger when playing Loyang, but that’s another story. [i](January 2012)[/i]

[b]Update[/b] - The mere fact I haven’t been playing Loyang since my original comment (almost four years ago) - and haven’t seen any of my euro-friendliest buddies play either for that - means this is a game I can as well do without: so during my Fall 2015 rating revise I lowered my rating from 7 to 6, which will hold until I’ll have the chance to re-evaluate it. Not too soon, it looks like.

RATING: 8
Only a couple plays, some solo, so far (and its great solo, btw) but I anticipate this being a big time keeper for me. As some have said, the action phase is pretty straightforward, and its the card phase that is the heart of creating your strategy. The scoring system is a clever way to make early money better than later, while removing money from the system, to keep it tight. For those iwth an issue with the scoring system, just think of leftover money, not as tie-breaker, but worth 1 point a piece, while steps on the path are worth 100 points.

RATING: 8
Two games.  Jury's still out.  On one hand I like it more than Agricola because you get the satisfaction of fulfilling orders, instead of the constant grind-without-reward feeling in Agricola.  So the game is more "fun".  And it's easier to plan ahead in Loyang; while I normally like strategic games less than tactical games, I prefer even less those strategic games in which players can easily mess you up!

However the game is much more dependent on card draws.  In Agricola, if I get bad cards, I could just ignore them and still have a chance, because they're optional goodies that cost to play.  You can't make money in this game without cards, and getting the right cards at the right time can mean much more success over your opponents.  My wife was shocked to see how far ahead I was in our second game -- the regular customers just fell my way.

I like it, but games like this (and Agricola) will always be situational choices, never my favourites.  For this game, the "situation" is that it's very good with two players, and has the making-or-buying-then-selling-and-earning-money theme that my wife enjoys a lot.

I will say that the game is almost completely solitaire-ish, which my wife and I prefer anyway.  I'm tempted to completely take out the cards that affect each other -- while that would make it 100% solitaire-ish, the benefit is that we could then take our turns at the same time and probably shave off 1/3 of the playtime.

RATING: 8.5
Loyang might make you neglect all the other games in your collection. The week after I got it, I played it every morning and night. That rarely happens, since I typically bring home 5 new games a week (it's research/product training for work, of course!).
I think of this as a gateway version of Agricola. Much of what makes that game so compelling is found here: Limited rounds, limited resources, limited cash, and an unlimited amount of things that you want to do. The key mechanical difference is that Loyang let's you take as many actions as you want per turn, as long as you can pay for them.
Luck of the draw is definitely stronger in Loyang. I've seen players make huge comebacks in the last few rounds due to receiving good hands while their opponents received nothing useful.
Watching my farm grow from a tiny pumpkin patch with a pathetic into a sprawling 8 field, money pumping monster
is one of the most satisfying experiences I've yet to have in this hobby.
An experienced player can teach this to a casual gamer in 5 minutes or less, another huge plus. 
Also a very good couples strategy game.

RATING: 5
So I have played Loyang about 5 times and I think I am done with it.  It's not a bad game by any means, but it's best played with no more than two and everything here is familiar to a veteran gamer, even the theme of growing vegetables.

The solo game works fine, but I am always skeptical of solo boardgames anyway, go play a video game or something.

For a two player game, the only real interaction is probably in the card drafting phase.  Here the game tricks you into thinking really hard about what to discard to the common pool, when in most cases your choices here have minimal effect.  The rest of the game is just optimizing your little farms, delivering vegetables and deciding what to plant.  You can play simultaneously for the most part.

It's all very enthralling while you play, but at the end you look up and see your score marker one space ahead.  You are the alpha farmer and all other pretenders must bow down to you.  Except that you just spent an hour pushing a little pumpkin token around while ignoring the person across from you.  How cool is that?

RATING: 7.5
Solo record 17+17.

An interesting game with more depth than I figured it would have.  This game is a huge departure from Agricola and Le Havre, but I think that's a good thing.  Thematically it's lame, you're growing and selling vegetables but the game itself is pretty fun. 

I'm finding the solo game to be very random at times.  If you get a lot of not particularly useful cards in the purchase area, it can be quite difficult to know what to grow and hard to get your engine running.  I dislike not knowing what to grow and what I'm going to need later and not being able to formulate an effective short term plan that bridges to a long term plan.  I don't have this issue with Le Havre or Agricola, so I'm not sure why this is bothering me so much in this game (despite it happening the last two games in a row).

My interest in this has dropped off quite a bit.  I need to get some two player games of this going, but I find that there are quite a few other games I'd rather play with my usual two player opponents.

Played a couple of games 2-player.  We both thought it was ok, but not deep enough, especially given that we can play a game of Agricola in the same amount of time.  There is part of a good game here, pity it did not take it the rest of the way.  I'll likely pull this out and play it from time to time, but it's not going to be a regular title.

I want to try this out with a few other people and see if I can capture the love for it.  If not, I guess it will hit the trade pile.

I ended up selling this after a few attempts to get it to the table.  I didn't even end up wanting to play it solo, so I sold it to make space for other games.

RATING: 3
OMG, if you thought LeHavre was bad, don't go anywhere near this. Agricola MKII it isn't ...

Feels like Jambo with all the fun sucked out ... take a nice quick intense 2 player game and add on a bunch of extra mechanics and rules, resulting in a game I'll be happy never to play again.

Apart from the dull but mind melting multiplayer solo type efficiency puzzle there is NOTHING you do in other player's turns, but you can't really plan in the downtime as there is enough interaction to screw up your plans. With fast game play I could play this sort of game - never going to be a favourite though, too mechanical, too 'solo' - but the downtime kills this one dead for me. I'll stick with Agricola.

RATING: 6
I can see where this was an early step along Uwe's path to Agricola and Le Havre.  There's some good ideas in here that the other two games do better.  Also the way the game handles casual customers drive me nuts.

*5/4/11* - In an effort to get more of the games sitting on our shelf played, my wife and I have been going through our collection looking for suitable 2p games.  The latest game on the table has been At the Gates of Loyang.

After a few 2p sessions I have to say AtGoL really shines with two.  Once the participants are comfortable with the rules the level of interaction goes up dramatically.  Instead of being so tightly focused on your own farm, you now pay attention to what your competition is doing.  That broadened awareness becomes a much bigger factor in what cards you take/play and actions you choose.

When we first purchased the game I rated AtGoL as the weakest of Uwe's "Harvest Trilogy" titles.  I think that had to do more with the wonky gameplay that 4p offers or the excessive downtime of 3p and 4p sessions.  With 2p the game displays its strength and offers a good combination of challenge/interactivity with the right length of playtime.

RATING: 7.5
Cada jugador es un comerciante que tiene que conseguir dinero para escalar en el track de puntos de victoria. Ese track llega hasta 20. En cada turno, puedes pagar 1 para escalar un puesto y después pagas según lo que te diga el track.
Cada turno tiene tres fases. Cosechar, repartir cartas y realizar acciones. En la cosecha consigues vegetales, con las cartas puedes conseguir ayudantes (personajes que te dan ciertas ventajas), clientes habituales, clientes eventuales o mercados. El juego consiste en ir meneando tus mercancías de la forma más óptima para conseguir dinero.
Es un juego de gestión de recursos en el sentido literal de la palabra. Bastante rompecocos. Se echa de menos un poco más de interacción con los otros jugadores. La unica pega que le veo es que es muy dificil contrarrestar una ventaja inicial (opino con sólo una partida jugada).
Recomendable sólo para jugones.

RATING: 6
I only played online on Yucata, where I like it, but I have doubts whether I would actually enjoy to play the cardboard version. There is very little interaction, and a lot to think about, so I guess there would be a lot of downtime. 

It's basically multiplayer solitaire, an optimization puzzle, with the notable exception of the action cards. This can lead to an unpleasant situation, where all the work spent on optimizing your trades can be ruined by a timely played action card of an opponent. In other words: I prefer games that only have indirect interaction, or games that are all the way confrontational. This one is in a weird spot in between.

The game is also very unforgiving. If you screw up a turn, you'll probably be behind for the rest of the game.

RATING: 8.5
Another wonderful farming game. Who'd have thought? Though the timing of harvests and future planning of turns can be very challenging, this feels much lighter and simpler than Agricola or Ora et Labora. I think it's partly because there is a push-your-luck/risk element absent from Rosenberg's other games: you might plant a vegetable knowing that you'll have an excess of it in a couple of turns, in the hopes that another customer will come along who wants it. The result is a game that is a little more unpredictable, and thus a little less strategic, than, say, Agricola. This might be frustrating to some people, but I think it's a nice change.

RATING: 9
AVAILABLE ON YUCATA!!!

Not as good as Agricola, but still really good. Each player is building up an economic engine by trading vegetables. The card distribution phase is quite interactive; the action phase is not (though the Con Man can still strike fear in your heart). Still, there's a wonderful race element as you try to keep track of how much your opponent has and keep up.

Faults? Yes. A little long (does it have to be 9 rounds?); a little fiddly; brain-burning and tough to reason through. And the number of tiny little rules you have to memorize! Yikes. But the game flows pretty smoothly after a few rounds, and I will definitely try this again.

UPDATE: 8 -> 9. This game is awesome. I find it more calculable than Agricola, but just as stressful.

RATING: 8
My initial play didn't strike me much, but it's grown on me. An important caveat though: my 8 rating is strictly for the 2-player game. I really think it degrades with each additional player: 3 players takes too long, and 4 takes too long *and* is too chaotic.

An interesting note on this game is that unlike the highly tactical Agricola or Le Havre, Gates of Loyang is as much risk management as it is planning or evaluation. On any given turn you don't have a ton of information, so you need to manage your fields and your cardflow in a way to keep your balance current revenues again keeping your options open, and know when to turn down a contract you can do because it'll constrain you too much for the future. It's clever, and the chance in the game is fun. But, it does mean that with 3 or 4 players, the added length is a real problem.

RATING: 5
Hrmm, I'm just not sure how I feel about this one, but I'm leaning toward the thought that I probably won't play this one a whole lot.  Half way through the game, I was really enjoying things.  However, as things progressed the game just seemed to go on and on and on with no sense of progression towards anything.  Every turn just felt like more of the same, and the game just plain overstayed its welcome.

The scoring system used here also seems strangely applied, and makes it a heck of a time for anyone to ever come up with a come from behind victory.  Seems like the leader is just going to stay there save a couple of really lucky card drafts for the trailing players and some terrible ones for the leader.

Also, while it has no bearing on my rating, I feel like this game is a complete rip-off for what you get.  I was sorely disappointed when I opened the $70 box to discover a cavern full of air, with a deck of cards and some vegimeeples thrown in.  Compare this to a Fantasy Flight giant box games like Descent which comes with junkloads of bits for under $70, and it just doesn't make sense.  After seeing what comes with the game, I would have expected Loyang to be published as an Alea mid-sized box with a $40ish price tag.  Buyer beware.

RATING: 7
Updated 7/17/10.

This is now my wife's second-favorite game.  That may be a little misleading actually.  I'm not sure she enjoys it as much as some other games, but she keeps requesting, it in part because she rarely wins.  We've only ever played 2-player, and given that the length of the game outpaces its interest to me, I doubt that we'd attempt a 4-player game more than once.  

Overall, At the Gates of Loyang is clearly the lightest and probably the weakest of Uwe Rosenberg’s Harvest Trilogy.  For the uninitiated, Loyang is a card-based board game about farming in ancient China. The core of the game is strategically planting crops for future harvests and collecting cards that allow you to exchange crops or sell crops for cash. As you can tell from that description, Loyang at times seems to share a closer bond with one of Rosenberg’s earlier games, Bohnanza, than it does with either Agricola or LeHavre. 

That said, Rosenberg has done an impressive job imbedding a risk/reward gamble in numerous facets of the game. For example, each crop has a value that is directly correlated to its scarcity, and the game employs an innovative victory point system that encourages players to purchase VP heavily each round at the risk of not having enough money to optimize future turns. The result is that numerous decisions each turn feel meaningful, which is rare in a card-based game with such a significant luck factor. 

The game is far from perfect, however. First, the resource-conversion bit is already AP-inducing enough that the second layer of short- and long-term balancing may just be too much for some people. (I can think of a few people already who I would never introduce this game to). Second, unlike my other favorite “get things to get things to build things or buy things” resource management games, Loyang provides only the slightest window for players to impact the decisions of those around them. In fact, the game that Loyang most reminds me of – Homesteaders – practically feels like a wargame in comparison, what with its confrontational “auction” mechanic and limited building types. If ever there was such a thing in our hobby as multi-player Solitaire, At the Gates of Loyang is it (Dominion is a close second and a apropos comparison). 

The result is a good game that’s lighthearted (if not light), and fun, but that feels like it occupies well-trod ground. I liked it enough to purchase it, and it serves us well as a whimsical optimization game for weeknights (specifically Fridays) with my wife (the bits are very attractive, which, alone, entices my wife to pull it off the shelf more often than it deserves).

Overall, it's a solid game that I can appreciate, but which I'll never be gaga over.  This type of simplistic resource optimization mechanic is not my cup of tea, but it comes in a pleasant and attractive package that makes for a fine 90-minute exercise.

RATING: 8
A good game from the creator of my favorite game, Agricola. Loyang isn't as good as that one or Le Havre, but it's nonetheless a good game which I enjoy. 

There is much more luck involved compared to his two major games; you can sow some pumpkins, but when the customer you draw wants turnips, you need to rethink what to do. There is a lot of card drawing, and especially the two-pack action is pretty random in my opinion as you might buy it for 2-3 gold and get nothing you want. Still, it's a decent game about serving your customers and earn the most money which you use to buy victory points.

The game can drag a little too much with 3 players as it's mostly a multiplayer solitaire game with little interaction, and you kind of need to wait for the first player to make his actions as he can have an impact on what you can do if he decides to use a helper. So I like it best with 2, but won't turn down a game with 3.

My recommended number of players: 2, 3

RATING: 3.5
From my comments on this list http://www.boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/48198/item/1093145?commentid=1304081#comment1304081


If you think of Agricola, Le Havre, and AtGoL as a (vegetable) series, then I would say this is a continuation more of the Le Havre route, but it's a bit more straightforward and less option laden.

However I do find it hard to get really enthused about pushing different coloured vegetables around. Sure for Agricola this was OK because the individual character attributes (occupations and minor improvements) you get so define where you're heading, and make everything more thematic. AtGoL does have helper cards which are kind of it's equivalent - but these are more one shot wonders and don't continue through the whole game making it resonate in the same way.

It does feel a slow, mathy, push things around, be an accountant kind of game and for me has so far given the impression that once more it's just not going to be fun long term. Unlike with Le Havre though it hasn't completely put me off on the first game play, but I'm not especially enthused so far.

Now with a four player game as well.

I wouldn't want to go near this game if anyone with AP was in the group.
It just isn't exciting. There's nothing that enthuses anywhere in this game. The gameplay works fine (although I have nagging suspicions that who gets the best cards, eg early fields in the auction and especially the two-packs is particularly significant), There isn't really a great deal of interaction, and the helpers that do interact feel forced to make there be interaction. The gameplay itself isn't particularly fun (manipulate vegetables to fulfil orders). The weird partnership action with 4 players is needed to ensure the game doesn't bog down but is really awkward and limits the interaction helpers even more.

My rating: halfstarnostarnostarnostarnostar

RATING: 4
In one regard, Loyang is the game that I wanted Agricola to be: you are farming, the harvest is central. This game, apparently, predates Agricola though, and draws more inspiration from Antiquity. As has been standard for Rosenberg in recent years, it's a rather complex little beast to explain, though it's actually very simple in play, in my opinion, too simple.

The real quick and dirty is that players have vegetable gardens that have between 3-9 spaces on them. They buy or trade for seeds that they plant and they harvest one item from each field at the start of each round. They then go through a phase where cards are dealt out from a deck of customers, traders, helpers. Beginning with a hand of 4, cards are discarded into the centre of the table, one by one. Once a player sees something that they want in the pile, they pick up that card, choose one other card from their hand, and throw the rest into the pile for the other people to choose from. After this, they go into their main turn where they can trade vegetables at market stalls and fill the needs of customers who can be regular or casual. Casuals pay more, but are one shots. Regulars offer consistent income that is easy to regularize with carefully planted fields but will commit drive-bys on your shop if you don't supply them every year, costing you $2 in repair bills. All of the money that is generated is used to buy VPs, where the first VP for the round always costs 1, and the extra ones always cost whatever the number is that you're landing on.

I like efficiency puzzles like this, and can take some joy in picking up huge piles of wooden pumpkin bits, but the thing that concerns me is that there is very little interaction between the players aside from a few of the helper cards, and that you see the entire deck of cards multiple times in each game. It's great fun to be picking up great piles of pumpkins, turnips, and ghostface killah (don't ask) and to turn them into the customers for cash, but it's not particularly variable and there's not a lot you can do to interact with the other players aside from the card-play phase. The problem there is that the very things that you would want to keep from them, namely customers that demand vegetables they can grow, are the very things that you can't afford to take because having unusable customers is not only a time sink but directly damaging to your score because of the penalties for not filling regular's orders, and for having too many casual customers queued up.

I also feel that the game is simply too long for what it offers. The narrative arc of rags to riches is there, and it's fulfilling to be pulling in big harvests at the end of the game and swapping veggies like a mad fool, but that back half really is, at a baseline, a lot like the front half, you're doing the same thing, just more of it, and not like Le Havre where there is a lot of chaining involved, it really is just "these two veggies go to this regular" in the front part of the game and "these two veggies go to this regular, those 2 to that regular, and those 3 to the casual" in the back half.

Revisiting this with 4 at the table basically reiterated the above points for me. The game was a bit lower on the downtime scale, which was good, but it wasn't as fresh either, so it seemed to be a rough wash for me. I never feel particularly creative when playing this game, as most of the decisions seem to be dictated by the cards that are dealt or made available to you. Making sure that you have enough veggies available throughout the game seems to be goal #1, and then getting the right customers to go with that are key #2. There is very little interaction in the game, which I don't care for, and the 2/4 player games have even less than the 3 in the use of helper cards, which doesn't improve it. I know the cardplay is supposed to offer interaction, but I don't feel like I'm having to make tough decisions in that phase all that often. What you're going to drop is usually obvious, as is what you're willing to pick cards up for. If you have a helper card that depends on poaching from someone else then you're going to want to wait for them to drop (provided you're in a spot in the order where you can force that) but for the most part there aren't any significant new decisions added to this phase for me regardless of the count.

I rated this a tentative 7 after the first play and it has settled, initially to a 6 and now to an honest 4. I expect a lot more out of games that are in this 1.5-2 hour window. If this game was about half as long I would definitely rate it higher, but as it is, it is way too limited for the amount of time that it takes up, and I don't see this ever clocking in at under 1hr20 except with 2 perhaps, and with 2 there are so many better options. Feels very repetitive to me.

Aside: don't ask me why, but for some reason we were on the gangster rap theme when playing this game. The white vegetable was ghostface killah, the two-pack was the Tupac, and the regular customers were committing drive-bys in retribution for not being served. This likely added .5 to my rating.

RATING: 7
Loyang was briefly on my Spiel-list when the game came out in 2009, but the hideous pricetag of €40 for a bag of veggiemeeples, two packs of cards, and some very minor cardboard work held me off almost instantly. In 2010 I could already see some suppliers dropping the price to a more acceptable €25. I eventually got an excellent (and hardly used) copy for €15 some time later. That price is probably too low again, but not by much.

It is a favourite of my partner, who really digs and loves the veggies, and so over time I clocked up a nice amount of games with our copy. I confess myself surprised at how the game has grown on me—it is an unashamed growth engine which I tend not to like much—and how insidious its mechanisms really are. The game at first appears to be nothing special really, and in fact quite often Uwe Rosenberg fans express serious disdain for Loyang when comparing it to Agricola and Le Havre, the two other titles of the so-called Harvest Trilogy. Loyang is admittedly the least involved of that trio, so if you are looking for that 'epic arc' or 'multitude of choices' you won't find them here, true. But that's not to say Loyang is without merits... and there is quite a simple test to find out if people know these: ask them [i]What is your highest score you've obtained to date?[/i]

It may sound anodyne to reduce a game to comparing high scores, but that misses the point. You see, Loyang is a growth engine [i]without brakes[/i]. There is no mechanism in the game which slows a player down in a periodic fashion. Like happens in, say, Agricola or Le Havre. Loyang encourages you to push things as hard and as far as they will go. It is not uncommon for beginning players to end up with a score of 15 or 16 VP plus some money left over, and wondering what the hubbub is about. But then you try for 17 VP, 18, all the way out to what at that point becomes a mind-boggling 21 VP. When playing for those stakes the slightest disturbance, the slightest error, the slightest miscalculation will wipe off many cash from your ledger and reduce your VP total. Suddenly what appear to be innocent and boring helper cards become laser-guided VP-seeking missiles of chaos and doom. That stupid gamey two-pack becomes a gamble which may or may not pay off magnificently. You will find ways to abuse even the simplest of helpers. Loans, so attractive at first, become more and more expensive the higher your VP total climbs, so the game expects you to do better more than averagely once you decide to accept the offer of 'free' cash. The whole struggle on a knife's edge only appears once you [i]really[/i] push the game. And the first inkling that the game has been pushed, is the highest score obtained to date. 

I will readily admit that the game has its moods and quirks: it can be argued that in such a difficult environment randomness really ought not to have a place. Yet it does, in various ways. So you cannot expect to reach your high score in every game. At the same time it should come as a comfort that you will not ever strike it so lucky that you can expect to boost your scores with an entire VP or so. The cards appear to deliver debilitating pinpricks rather than massive punches to the face. But given enough pinpricks, you'll still suffer. Also, despite what it says on the box this is a 2 player game exclusively. You can play with more people as the material is there in the box, but I fail to see the point. You need surgical precision to take out opponent's cards, and can do without the complication of a 3rd and 4th player.

All in all, an unjustly shunned game by Uwe Rosenberg. It may be simple in concept, but the devil is truly in the details with this one. I'm glad that it eventually made it back to print in 2016, despite remaining hellishly expensive as when it first came out.

My top score is 18+4, by the way. Not as good as can be (as I said I'm not good with growth engines) but still sufficiently respectable to speak with a modicum of experience on what makes this game 'tick'. And to wonder how the [i]hell[/i] people have managed to break the 20 VP barrier.

RATING: 7
John says that if I love Antiquity I will love Loyang... but I don't.  There just isn't enough variety here to make this a long term winner.  Pick some cards, hope the combos work and plant some crops.  Then rinse and repeat.

The player interaction is flimsy at best.  You should pay attention to what others are doing, but only to keep optimizing your own position.  The fact that you can actually effect other players negatively is actually a downer in this game as it comes almost unexpectedly as there are very few cases where that is the best move. So when it does happen it seems a bit malicious.

But either way there is some challenge here.  Try to figure out the best way to feed people.  How to change goods into other goods.  How much to pay for points.  Yeah it feels done, but it is a decent remix.

But if you can, play someone else's copy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
What Rosenberg has created here is a game that builds on his familiar theme of agriculture, but is much simpler to learn.  Players plant vegetables, harvest them, and then sell them in order to get more.  Regular customers are demanding, while casual customers give only a one-time payoff.  Various helper cards make things easier, and market stalls allow you to swap vegetables to get what you need.

For such a simple game, the mental gymnastics required are substantial.  This is a great cerebral game for anyone that likes a good puzzle, as you try to figure out how to get the most out of your meager fields.  Is it worth it to buy a leek to plant it? Or are you better off trading for cabbage?

Loyang is one of those games that just clicks with me and it's hard to describe why I'm so fond of it.  It just WORKS.  I've fallen very hard for it.

RATING: 8.7
4th game in Uwes 'trilogy'. I consider this to be Uwe's sleeper. The wife and I have played it so many times that this is the first game that I 1) have had to get card protectors for and 2) have considered buying a new game-JUST for a 'card replacement'. Simple yet really fun. A good amount of strategizing and also a good bit of luck-will my next field be a 3,4,5, or 6? The larger the field the fewer crop types you can plant there AND the lower the worth of the crops. The 'helpers' add lots of flavor and the initial play to the 'court' (or is that the 'El Grande' terminology?) is fraught with stress....because you have to take ONE from the court and ONE from your hand-but in order to have a good one in the court-you have to lay it there....meaning that in the intervening turn, opponents are likely to take it....wait too long and you may get stuck with good ones in your hand that you cannot use. EXTREMELY interesting/novel scoring mechanism too-you buy points. The first one is always just $1, but for each higher point you have to pay THAT point value (so for example if you are on 15, going to 16 is just $1 but going to 17 is $1 + $17)....as with the bulk (all?) of Uwes games-you can take a loan. Loans in this game are unlimited, can be taken at any time, used for anything-and sometimes its even smart to do so! But they can NEVER be repaid!! You just lost 1 point at the end of the game for each loan (HINT: Take a look at the # of loans your opponent has)..... 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
Games are supposed to be enjoyable.  This game is not.  This game would be panned by board game critics (oh wait, there aren't any . . .) if it weren't for the name "UWE" on the box.  Specifically:

(1) It's the epitome of multi-player solitaire.  Yea there are some helpers that interact with other players, but they mostly suck and don't get used often.  In other words, you never really plan with them in mind.
(2) It's too long. I don't think this game has ever taken less than 2 hours, even with 2 players.  
(3) Each turn involves a lot of mental math, and thus AP. And guess what? Players usually can't do actions simultaneously, meaning that you're going to wait while your opponent (or you) tries to squeeze out 1 more cent on their turn.
(4) Games are always close, which means squeezing that last cent out of your vegetables might actually be important, thereby justifying the AP. It also means that fortunate draws can cause a win amongst experienced players.  

So it's a long, AP-inducing, multiplayer solitaire game. And mind-numbingly boring. If you want to Rosenberg it up, play Agricola instead. Or Ora et Labora. Heck I'd even recommend Le Havre over this.  

Bottom line: It's too similar to much better games to be worth your time.

RATING: 8
If only real vegetable farming were this easy! Seems quite a bit more strategic with the Regular Customers.  Downside is the AP-ness of trying to be super-efficient (and becoming familiar with the various cards).  I actually enjoy this game quite a bit and would like to play it more often.  These Rosenberg games have wonderful components and artwork to compliment the great gameplay. Unlike Agricola and Le Havre, there is no mandatory feeding of workers/family. Just plant and harvest vegetables to sell to customers and use the proceeds to buy VPs. Add in some market stalls so you can more easily manipulate what vegetables you have and some Helper cards to change some of the rules and that's pretty much the game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Picked this game up for a steal, without knowing anything about it. I was resistant to buy it for that reason, but am glad I was talked into it.

This game has great depth and complexity and will require careful and considered foresight. 

It's a game of economics: select customers to serve; buy vegetables; invest the vegetables in plowed fields; harvest vegetables; seek to fulfill customer orders.

It sounds simple, but really it is not. Veggies that return the most money only have a low yield from cropping. Not all veggies can be grown in all fields. Some customers want very difficult to fulfill orders. 

This game scales flawlessly, and works well as a solo game.

It gets a high rating because of its depth compared to its good length of play (circa 1hr or less with 2p) and its replayability.

RATING: 7
Gets an extra point for the drafting mechanics which I really enjoy.  But I see this maxing out at 8.  I like the card interactions and look forward to more plays.  The negative that I see is the scoring system.  It seems too tight.  Everyone is doing different things to make money and score points, but ultimately the winner will likely be tied with other players at game's end.  This isn't necessarily bad, but it can feel strange for some reason.

Dropping it down a point (8-->7).  I still like it, but there are some issues that I'm seeing. The main issue is that the end gets overly fiddly in terms of optimizing the use of your vegetables, 2-packs, and other cards. It's very difficult to track what the other players are doing.  Plus with a myriad of "take-backs" and "redo's" to optimize, it drags the game out a bit too much.  As a result I lose interest in following what another player does on his turn.  Thus it can lull you into the solitaire mindset, until that one moment when someone hoses you with a card.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Pre-ordered October 13, 2009. 

The third in Uwe Rosenberg’s Harvest Trilogy, At the Gates of Loyang has few similarities with the other games in the trilogy beyond having a harvest phase and the presence of excellent looking veggiemeeples. It is less interactive than either Agricola or Le Have; the decisions one makes in Loyang rarely have an impact on the other players, except during narrow slices of the game. It is also lighter than either of those games without being especially shorter. 

When boiled down to its essentials, Loyang is a slightly interactive efficiency puzzle. Because of this, the game loses very little when played solo. In fact, that might be my favorite form of the game, though the two player version of the game is fast enough and interesting enough to be worth playing. 

I have since traded this one away, but will probably reacquire it at some point due to my girlfriends enjoyment of the game. Boy, was she mad when I traded it.

RATING: 8
An excellent farming simulation. The theme is great and I really enjoyed building my engine. This game can be very AP prone you have to watch out with whom you are playing with. Best as two or three player game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Players are vegetable growers, planting and sowing crops, then trying to sell them for cash coins.  Coins are spent on prestige, which is how you win the game.  Fairly simple game, rules-wise, where players harvest a good from their planted fields, pick up a few cards via a slightly strange distribution mechanism, then take actions to process their vegetables.  At the end of each turn, cash is used to buy advances on the prestige track.  Fun game, let down by a rather poor set of rules along the lines of Agricola and Le Havre, but once you crack the code you're fine.  The game seems designed for very close finishes, usually decided by tie breakers.  Probably best as a solo or two player game.  Very expensive for essentially a box of air, a deck of cards and some wooden pieces.

RATING: 6.75

Uwe needs to simplify and make the accessible, middle-weight masterpiece the gaming world knows he is capable of.

RATING: 7
[-]I have owned this forever, but it seems everyone is past playing it.  I need to get this one under my belt and I refuse to solo it.[/-]

After a bajillion years of having this on my shelf, I finally got this played, twice in the same day.

First off, there is something absolutely fun about building the best economic machine you can, so even losing isn't that bad.  With the draw of the cards, though, there's no guarantee that what you're building will work every game, which makes it a great euro for me.  No optimal path means lots of variety.

However, never play this game 4 players.  While the 'partner' rules are there to speed gameplay along, this game is absolutely best with 2 players.  We played with 3 as well and it takes away the partner thing, but having that extra player just made the game drag on a bit.  With a 2 player game, we managed to blast through the rounds and the scoring was extremely close.

As a final note, cash is EVERYTHING.

RATING: 7.5
It's like the Occupation and Sowing/Harvesting parts of Agricola expanded into their own game.  It reminds me a lot of 51st State, in that the goal for much of the game is to find ways to spend the resources you already have rather than to find the resources you need to spend.  I like the resource management part of the game, but the card drafting at the beginning is often quite frustrating, especially because a large portion of the deck is "Helper" cards that are only situationally useful.  Like 51st State, it's very necessary to draw as many cards as possible.  Also, the way turns are structured makes the game feel somewhat slow, as each player spends a good 10 minutes deliberating their turn during the drafting phase, and then takes a long turn of resource swapping and spending.

RATING: 6
At the Gates of Loyang is the purest refinement of Rosenberg's familiar reap-and-sow mechanic. The key aspect of the game is establishing an oiled supply chain to support your growing customer base. Properly planting these fields, and securing appropriate customers provides the game's challenge, but he most interesting facets of Loyang lie outside of this core mechanic. The recurring action card draft, though unique, deserved more focus. Additionally, the balancing of regular and casual customers introduces an element of prioritization, but from a point generating perspective often results in a zero-sum benefit.  Loyang has a decent narrative arc, but varies little from game to game. Players first build up their infrastructure, maintain steady production, and finally cash everything out for points. While reap-and-sow is an enjoyable mechanic, Loyang is a bit too long, and could have benefited from a condensed set of rounds.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
It's easy to see this is the game that Agricola would have been if Caylus didn't come along. It's no surprise this game was supposedly designed before Agricola but released after words - it's just not nearly as good as Agricola for a lot of reasons. First, the interaction is fiddly and uninspiring. Loyang's cards lack the balance of cards Agricola - there's just some that do things that are rather unbalanced with no downside or prerequisites. Above all, Loyang is a lot longer with a lower payout. Simply put, this game was obsolete the day it was released.  I'm suspicious that if the Agricola phenomenon never happened, this game would have never have been released.

RATING: 10

-------------Original rating 30/11/15 Score:8
[b]Rating based on - 15+ plays, 1 player and 2 player[/b]
This is the first Uwe Rosenberg I've played/owned. Best thing ever - vegimeeples! Other than that, the game plays really smoothly as both a solo and 2 player effort. Love the way the cards are laid out in the solo game. Tried an open market like that in the 2 player game a few times but it induced an unbelieveable amount of AP in my opponent :) Scores are slightly less than in the normal game because of having to pay for items in the market, but I like the element it adds of having to make sure you don't hand your opponent too many easy opportunities. My best score so far is 18.18 and I hope to continue improving. There is that element of luck - sometimes all you have is helpers, sometimes you have a handful of regular customers and not enough veggies - but I love the puzzle of figuring out what to do in those circumstances with the resources at your disposal.
[b]Status: KEEPER[/b]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.3
Personally, I really enjoy this game. I was surprised to see a relatively low rating for this game compared to some of Uwe's other notable titles, but this game is great. It's essentially multiplayer solitaire that can get a little cutthroat only if a player really goes out of his or her way to do so by stealing or using another player's cards but most of the time they will have access to everything they need. It's fascinating how Uwe was able to balance a game so incredibly well that an average game will be 16-17 points, an extremely well played game will be 18 points, a borderline perfect game will be 19 points, and 20 points seems nearly impossible. It's a very relaxing puzzle that requires brilliance. Very nice to play after a long day. Highly replayable.

RATING: 6
Played 2 times with 2 and 3 players for 3.0 hours.
Rating history: 7,6

7- APR 2010: Very Euro (especially for Uwe considering his recent designs) but a lot of unique mechanisms and arrangement in the player space. Still, point salad, right?

6- JUN 2014: Since I've been on quite the Uwe Rosenberg kick lately, I wanted to give this one another go - but my feelings from last session (see above comment) from four year ago are simply redoubled. Very euro, very point salad, but - worse - very parallel play. And the mechanical stunts you can pull kill the theme for me completely (emptying out a small market by swapping 1:1:1:1 makes no sense, nor does bringing in occupations then releasing them with no use OR penalty!) The prettiness of the visuals and the farming theme are seductive, and make me want to give this a 7, but if I'm being honest about GAME enjoyment, this is a 6. (Which begs an interesting question - if the visuals are so appealing that it sort of draws you to the game - should you give it the extra point?? )

Take Jambo and Agricola (both very good games), then take the worst, artificial parts of goods swapping in Jambo, and the worst, rushed pressure farming of Agricola, and you get my feelings for Loyang.

RATING: 8.2
Personally, I like this game better than Agricola.  This game has fewer text cards to keep track of at one time. 

I like the loan mechanic--you can't pay them back, they simply count against you at the end of the game.  The card choosing mechanic is different, and perhaps the most confusing part of the game, although hardly difficult.

the game moves quickly & because it is a set number of turns, easy to know how long the game will last.

I am sorry it took so long to get this to the table.  How a box looks often determines how quickly we get to it--and I am not drawn to the box "looks" or colors of Loyang.  The components are really nice.

The one drawback is that if you get behind early on in the game, it is very difficult to catch up.  If someone gets a few points ahead early in the game it is likely they can stay there unless they make a mistake.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This is a game with a hearth full of math; certainly much more abstract that its cute theme would suggest.

[u]Overview[/u]
The players are farmers who produce and sell vegetables. During every of the nine turns, each of the fields the players have planted produces one vegetable which can be sold, planted, exchanged for other vegetables or be left in the storehouse. The players can play helpers (cards giving special bonuses), merchant stalls (where it's possible to exchange vegetables), regular customers (who need the same two vegetables for four turns) and occasional customers (who want a set of three specific vegetables); when played, these are added to the players' board. Every turn it's also possible to purchase a two-pack (two random cards), take loans (which are not paid back, but simply decrease the loanee's score) and spend money to increase prosperity (prosperity - loans = victory points).

[u]Strategy and comments[/u]
It is important to rapidly expand production, aiming for at least 7 new vegetables per turn, to satisfy two regular and one occasional customers. This maximizes the revenue from the occasional customers and is worth at least a couple of prosperity levels each turn. Players shouldn't forget to keep planting new fields to maintain their influx of vegetables; not paying attention often causes a severe decline in production during the second half of the game. Flexibility and differentiation usually pay off: you'll often have to manage without the cards you really need and producing every vegetable (or at least most of them) means having to rely less on market stalls. Seeing as many cards as possible improves the chances of finding what one needs, so keeping down the price of two-packs and buying them every turn can be very beneficial. Loans seem worth it only if they let you expand your production.

There seem to be too many cards, at least for two players; this makes it rather unlikely, during the card "drafting" phase at the beginning of each turn, to find what you need. The two player version would probably be more strategic and enjoyable with a smaller deck and a little more structure, for example with a couple smaller decks to add to the rest of the cards after 3 and 6 turns, giving the game three distinct phases (focus on expansion, extra regular clients and additional occasional clients). The interactive cards are a good addition, as they gently remind the players to check what their opponent is doing and to ponder over ways to screw them.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Loyang is quite different from what I expected it to be after the first two titles of the harvest trilogy. Players grow vegetables to sell them to customers and make the most money.

Even though there is some thematic overlap with Agricola it plays very differently. In fact, it is very much multiplayer solitaire. There are, however, a few interactive elements, and because of those players cannot all play simultaneously, leading to serious downtime.

The game is almost entirely tactical since you cannot plan further ahead than a turn or maybe two. At the core, it's a game for optimizers, and that makes some of the interactive cards all the more puzzling: In a game where players sometimes spend 10 minutes per turn slogging to the optimal vegetable delivery chain because a single coin more or less can matter quite a bit, cards that can then simply steal stuff from an opponent seem very much out of place. The injection of interactivity in general is commendable, but this? Seriously?

I like the general idea, and it's quite possible that one should look at it as more of a two-player game, really, but with more it's a bit overly long and drags more than it should.

RATING: 8
Loyang is a pleasant and relatively simple execution of Rosenberg's Harvest mechanic and it works well in the context of this game. Things always feel tight, and one must work hard to edge out over their opponent, which adds a lot of tension.  There is enough to worry about with fulfilling regular customer orders and the like, but there is also enough easy-going stuff so that the game feels less stressful than Agricola, for example.  The workers adds a lot of variety to the game. Too bad this game never figured a real solid 3- or 4-player ruleset, but I think this is still my favourite 2-player Rosenberg, even thinking of later 2-player versions of Agric and Le Havre.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
What a surprise this has turned out to be.  Only 2 solo games so far, but it is much, much, much better than expected.  The components are great and the game looks like Agricola, but I think I enjoy this one more.  There is some luck involved with the cards, but so what?  There is still plenty of room for strategy; look carefully, and you will see that a bad first move might cripple you, but careful adjustments later on can save your skin.

This game keeps getting better.  It seems like you need to generate an early engine churning out as many vegetables as you can each round, but that doesn't guarantee success.  If you don't get the right customers, and are missing that one crucial market stall or helper, money can get awfully tight very quickly.  I've had a game I thought was lost turn around with some fortuitous customers, while I've had other games humming along smoothly, only to be derailed by lack of good customers.  

I wonder if this game is improved at all with other players, or if solo is the way to go.

Played with 4 players - and it was fantastic.  Yes, it does have that Princes of Florence solitaire feeling, but there is some interaction during the card phase, and really, the game is so good that it never felt boring.  This is a must-have.

After a 2-player game, I love it even more.  I got killed by a first-time player, but that made it an even better experience.  This game might be the perfect combination of brain-burning puzzle with just the right amount of randomness (cards and fields).

It's up to the full 10 rating now.  We just played a 2-player game in a little over an hour.  With a few more plays, this could easily get down to 45 or 50 minutes.  I managed to hit 17 points with 9 cash left over, but ran out of steam at the end.  There is so much variability in how the game plays depending on the cards, your chosen strategy, and even a bit of what your opponents do.  Buying extra points early is the key, as they get prohibitively expensive later in the game.

RATING: 7.5

Update:  I played this game, on back to back days with my wife.  It's fun and enjoyable.  She likes games where it doesn't feel like we are fighting and "competing", and this game definitely doesn't feel like that.  You are largely taking care of your own business, and it's hard to screw someone else over in a big way.  There are minor interactions, and you can definitely try to do that, but by and large, it's a game that feels like you are tending your own vegetable patch.

I'm not super interested in playing the game with 4 players, as I think it would just get long.  We played our second game in an hour, which is what this game feels like it should be with two people.  If playing with 4, I think you would need to play the actions phases simultaneously or something, in order to speed up the game a bit, otherwise, you are looking at a 2 hour deal for a game without the weight and depth that sort of timeframe warrants.

One thing though - for a similar price, both MSRP and actual from amazon/miniature market, it's much less components than are found in Agricola.  I think I would just expect more pieces for a game in that price range, or to for it to have a lower price tag.  While I bought it for 20, and am fine withe price, it just felt a little light for a $60 MSRP.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The more I play the more I like this game. At first seemed fairly luck oriented due to random card draws every round, but I now realize with good selection and some strategic planning you can shape your luck.  Enjoyable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
At the Gates of Loyang is what I call a mini-puzzle game.  Each turn presents you with a tactical mini-puzzle that you must solve.  This category would include games such as Antiquity, Factory Fun, Mr. Jack, RoboRally, Babel, Goldland, Expedition, Uruk, Key Harvest, Java, and Jambo.  You must efficiently chain several small actions in order to shuffle around vegetables and fields and money and cards.  Money is extremely tight, and that's what makes the game work.  There is a constant tension between earning immediate cash and investing for future turns.  The reason for the tension is that you buy victory points at the end of each turn, and points purchased early in the game are much cheaper than points purchased late.

I like the game best by far with two players.  Why bother keeping track of three other players when almost everything you do doesn't involve them at all?  The four-player game is a little weird in that you pair off into two parallel two-player universes each turn, but does also allow a broader selection of cards to pass through the game - which is nice if you're looking for something in particular.  I avoid playing with three because there is simply too much downtime.

There is very little interaction among the players in Loyang,  Whether this is a bug or a feature depends on your tolerance/enjoyment of very solitaire-ish games.  For example, it's significantly less interactive than Le Havre, Agricola, Race for the Galaxy, and Dungeon Lords, and a bit less interactive than most games of Dominion.  I would rate it equal to Factory Fun or Galaxy Trucker in level of player interaction.

To me, Loyang does not feel similar to Agricola or Le Havre.  Instead, it wouldn't be too crazy to think of Loyang as Antiquity: The Card Game.  There's a very clear lineage from Antiquity which the designer makes explicit in his notes.  It's most obvious in the harvest mechanism, but it goes beyond that to include the entire feel and style of the game.

[Update] At the Gates of Loyang has gotten a LOT of play as a solitaire game. In part, this is because I seem to like the game more than my gaming neighbors. But mostly, it's because this is such an outstanding solitaire game. The weakest part of the multiplayer game is the card drafting phase. In the solitaire game, this is replaced by a matrix-like card display where you can see and manipulate coming attractions. The card matrix allows you to plan ahead better, and makes the puzzle-solving aspect of the game trickier and more interesting.

I currently would rate the game:
9 for 1 player
8 for 2 players
5 for 3 players (takes WAY too long)
6 for 4 players

RATING: 8
I don’t see the latest three games by Uwe Rosenberg as a trilogy as they are all quite different. The most striking similarity between them is that they are all fiddly. That is however not something I am concerned with. Neither am I concerned that the game is a little solitaire.

I give heads up that the game is one of the few games that really play well solo, which is good because I don’t think it will get much love from my regular gaming group. It has a well-executed theme and lovely art and components.

RATING: 5
If you only consider the idea, the mechanic, the graphic design and how the game works "technically", Loyang is definitely a 8 or 9 since it is designed flawlessly.  However, even the most perfect design can be boring, tiring or simply annoying.

This game can only be described as "Multiplayer solitaire with considerable downtime".  This is no longer fun, it's work, it's work which you do alone, and it's painful work if you have a player in your group who plays slowly. I don't see why I should sit down for 2-3 hours with some friends, not talking at all and twisting my brain on how to optimize my food production and sales.  One of our players said yesterday: "Next time I'd suggest that everybody plays at home alone.  And when you're done you phone your buddies and tell them your final score."

Uwe Rosenberg is surely one of the most intelligent game designers of our time, but his games have recently become more and more autistic.  Bohnanza is a brilliant masterpiece of interaction.  Agrcicola is also excellent but already has a considerable solo aspect which even increased with Le Havre. But with Gates of Loyang he has created a board game which reduces the entire idea of gaming to absurdity.  

COME ON!  GAMING IS A SOCIAL ACTIVITY!

Edit: Raised rating fom 2 to 5 because I have to admit that Loyang is a pretty good solo puzzle.  I got a copy for 10 Euro at the Spiel 2012 in Essen and I'm actually enjoying it.  However, I still do not consider this a game in the sense of something you want tomplay together with your friends.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I must say I have been very pleased with Loyang.  There are constant decisions to be made, and most of them are tough.  There seem to be a multitude of options and strategies to pursue, and none of them seem to be foolproof or formidable.  For the most part, players can execute their plans and strategies without an abundance of interference from their opponents.  There are some helpers that do allow some tampering with an opponent's markets or cards, and while these are usually troublesome, they aren't devastating.  Further, as mentioned earlier, cards which affect an opponent only affect one's "partner".  While some have complained that this gives the game a Solitaire feel, I disagree.  There is enough interaction not only by the use of these cards, but also in the card selection process.  I feel there is just enough to keep matters interesting, but not intrusive.

The most significant problem I have with the game is its length.  Each game I've played has lasted three hours or more.  I've mentioned many times previously that I don't mind longer games, but Loyang seems to drag a bit in the middle rounds.  The game would seem to maintain more if its excitement if it could be played in two hours or so.  Some groups might be able to accomplish this, but we have not come close to that mark.  However, the somewhat excessive duration has not been enough to force me to abandon the game.  Every game I have played has been tense and exciting, with one even being decided via a tie-breaker (most cash).  There is a growing sense of anxiety as the game progresses as you try to pull your plan together and squeeze out a few more coins to advance just one more space on the prosperity track. Loyang continues to intrigue, which keeps me going back in time for yet another rich experience at the gates of this ancient city.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
On my first play of this game, I found the number of decisions to be made a little overwhelming.  Do I want to keep this card or that one?  If I discard it, what can someone else do with it if they take it?  What do the special cards do?  Will I be able to attack someone with this card, and does it matter whether I go last/2nd-last and get to choose a victim?

Planting lots of fields that match the demands of your regular customers seems to be the name of the game.  But after 4 successful rounds, your regulars are fulfilled, and you have to change your economy to grow something else, so that can be a challenge.  And the challenge is different depending on how many rounds are left at the point that a supply field or a demand card is depleted.

I should play this again to get a better feel for how to win, but I'd rather play many other games instead (including several other games by Uwe Rosenberg).  And frankly, the luck factor of the card draw is too high -- if you get a great helper (or set of helpers), or draw the right demand card at the right time, you end up with extra resources that can help you disproportionately to other players, which is just not fair.  At least in Agricola and Le Havre, the randomness is limited to the beginning draw, not renewed throughout.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
A brain burner with a lot of down time.  Essentially a game of resource swapping... Way too long for what it is (over 2.5 hours + 30 minutes of rules explanations).

Card selection mechanic is interesting but does not alleviate the randomness issue.  Essentially you need to wait and hope that someone will lay down the card you are looking for.  If you have two good cards in hand, you have to take a chance and lay one down and hope no one selects it so you may select it on your next pass...  A weird approach for a card draft mechanic.

Once a player falls behind, there is next to no chance of getting back into the game, as the interaction is somewhat limited (and convoluted with 4-players and the aweful "partner" rule, where you may only interact, negatively, with the "partner" selected).

Score: 19-19-17-15
For the player at "15", he/she needed 16+17 (30) coins to join 3rd place, and another +18+19 (70) to tie with first...  When you are generating a little over 20 per turn, you are somehow 3 turns behind the leaders, and are not generating enough cash to have a chance to catch up.  Loans could work, but the cost renders it useless as the price is a FULL point PER 5 coins at the END of the game, per card.  OUCH.

In a 4-player game, the "Con Man" card is next to impossible to target, as a quadruple planet alignment needs to occur first.  This card works better in a 3-player game, where there is no "partner" limitation.

Don't expect to play this again, and canceled my order.

RATING: 8
These days Uwe has been releasing a lot of designs that just don't appeal to me. Many have had spatial elements or been totally overwrought. The harvest trilogy is my pick for Uwe's best games, and Loyang is definitely the best of the bunch for solo play. While my favorite of the series is Le Havre, I think each game has an excellent complexity/payoff ratio. They get the gears turning without needing constant rulebook references, they move at a good pace, and they reward repeated plays.

Loyang is particularly good for solo play because it is not just an engine builder where you are snowballing, but it's an optimization game where you need to take risks early on to get a good score by the end of the game. Order fulfillment, production management, and a bit of card play all come together to make this a wonderful meaty euro 

Best with 1-2

RATING: 7
A pretty simple game in comparison to Uwe Rosenberg's last two releases. Sow fields, harvest the vegetables, then try to fulfil the orders of customers who visit your stall. A sizeable luck element in what cards you're dealt, especially in the 'two pack' card purchase mechanism. I'm not sure 9 rounds were really necessary, as an optimisation game it's vulnerable to overthink, so it can stretch to 2 hours+ which is bordering on overstayed welcome.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a charming game that presents players with lots of things they can do to generate more stuff and a lot of control over their turn.  I like that a lot, and the components are absolutely irresistible.  However, I must admit that the game has some flaws.  The interaction is very low--I'm tempted to just play it in solitaire mode instead--and the downtime is high, but most disappointingly, it's entirely possible for players to win and lose before the end of the game. So the end in particular is kind of a letdown, but the beginning and middle are great.  

For what it's worth, later plays were more even among players.  Oddly, there is much less downtime for four players than for three. (3 plays)
