RATING: 7.25
Es un juego medio-pesado de gestión económica con algo (o bastante, según opiniones) de azar en las cartas. En tres palabras "puzzle de optimización". Ocupa un lugar inferior a sus dos hermanos mayores (Agricola y Le Havre) en la trilogía de las cosechas, pero no por ello es mal juego, ya que a fecha de hoy estamos hablando que no supera a los juegos que ocupan el nº2 y el nº 6 del ránking de bgg. Es el más táctico y el más ligero de los tres, ya que estás limitado a 4 cartas distintas por turno que te impiden hacer cálculos a largo plazo. Buena idea la  del "draft de cartas", que dificulta tener siempre tu jugada favorita. También es interesante la mecánica de los clientes (una especie de comensales de un restaurante a quienes debes servir los vegemeeples pues su paciencia es limitada y si les hartas, te penalizarán) PROS: "Vegemmeples", más corto que sus hermanos mayores. Funciona mejor que los otros dos en solitario y a dos jugadores. CONTRAS:  Excesivamente matemático para lo que ofrece, lo que favorece el A/P. Elevado "downtime" a más de 2 jugadores. Parece que jugar a 4 jugadores es forzar demasiado el sistema. ¿Dura 9 turnos y te putearán en dos de ellos y no podrás hacer nada? No hay traducción oficial al español: todo en inglés.

RATING: 6
After a few times through, I'm a bit conflicted on this one.  On the one hand, I do enjoy the game system - though it's definitely more towards the puzzle end of the spectrum, it's a very enjoyable puzzle that has a bit of that ol' Agricola time crunch (though to a much lesser extent).  On the other hand, the extreme lack of player interaction brings down the game to a surprising degree.  After one game my wife remarked, "why not just play the solo version?" and I don't really have a good answer for that (though the endgame did see a little bit of cross-player screwage on both our parts).  I really wish Mr. Rosenberg had found a way to integrate a little more player interaction into the design.

RATING: N/A
[2016-11] Игра Уве Розенберга "про урожай".

из обзора:
Пропитание как расплата за развитие наступает в конце каждого раунда в «Агриколе» и «Гавре». Над игроками висит дамоклов меч расплаты за недостаточное усердие в этом вопросе. Это заставляет разделять свою тактику на два фронта: добудь пропитание и постарайся сделать это максимально эффективно, чтобы побольше действий осталось на непосредственное развитие фермы (постройку зданий/кораблей). По этому фактору игровой процесс «Лояна» выделяется более простой формулой цели игрока: больше денег и никаких дополнительных стремлений. Возможно, именно поэтому в среде искушенных и опытных игроков эта игра не достигла высших строчек рейтинга: слишком проста.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
A charming economic game with relatively relaxed competition. The pleasant tactility of sowing and harvesting the wooden crops in your fields is appealing. 

(I do wish the crops had been tailored to the setting rather than re-used from Agricola; it's easy enough to call the wheat "rice," but it's harder to ignore the pumpkins--a New World plant somehow in Han Dynasty China. Minor gripe, I know.)

It has a lot more luck than you might expect from what seems like a classic Euro, and as such many of the decisions feel reactionary rather than planned out (though I could just be inexperienced with it). That limited ability to form strategies ahead of time means it's not the sort of game you'll think about when you're not playing it, but still it seems like something I'd rarely turn down if it were suggested.

RATING: 7.5
No doubt,  a good game.  Rosenberg does not disappoint.

Each round you try to solve 'the puzzle' (earn the most of money) and it's challenging, but what's really interesting is the interaction with other players (using their resources, blocking them, messing up with their plans).  Interestingly some people don't see this interaction... Odd.

The game is definitely on tactical (rather than strategic) side and that (among other things) make it very different from Agricola/Le Havre.  Hence it's hard to say about 'Rosenberg's trilogy', despite some people make this notion...

WARNING: 4-player game is basically unplayable - too long (despite playing in pairs) and too complex.  By the latter I mean that you are unable to study _exactly_ what's going in other players' tableaux.  And you cannot plan/predict interactions (->Card Phase as well as using helpers), because of way too many variables.  And very precise planning is absolutely crucial in this game...

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Might be the weakest of the three sure but look at the games you are comparing this too, very strong games so keep that in perspective.

Essentially a contract filling engine game with a similar theme to its brothers that is once again strongly integrated.  There is a new mechanic here where everyone starts their turn with four cards but will only keep one. The first player plays one of the cards into a "common pool", next player can take a card from the pool and keep one of his own but then adds the rest of his hand to the pool and "bows out" OR just add one to the pool and kind of pass.  This continues until each player ends up with two cards, one from the pool and one from his hand, holding out to the end gives you a small? advantage to go first so just a tad push your luck maybe. This works fairly good overall and feels fresh. 

Long term planning is not really possible here like in Gric or Le Havre (anyone that thinks Agricola does not have long term planning doesn’t fully grasp the game or just chooses to ignore it, its there in spades [order of cards from get-go and order of farm built adjusting all on the fly]) but there is a little.  Here you just want to plant as much veggies as possible in order to give you more options and in order to sell as much as possible for more money to buy more points.  You don’t know really WHAT to plant though sometimes as future customers come up randomly BUT there seems to be a lot of everything so I don’t think you’ll get too screwed, you just cant make too specific of plans, still a meaty game though. So the game is largely tactical and might offer a bit less and due to this should maybe be a bit shorter to keep its appeal, it would be nice to keep it to 90 minutes or 2 hours max with 4 players, 2 hours should be possible. 

More luck here than the other games unless you dont draft in Gric which I think would be silly, you just have to draft unless you really LOVE luck. Here which customers you get and how many you get are somewhat random, when the game comes down to 6 dollars for a win, this wont be too satisfying and this burnt someone just like that in one game near the end. There are also extra fields that come out which can help you BIG and then there is the "market bitch" as I dubbed her. I needed the veggies from my market stall for a contract and the market bitch lets another player steal my whole market stall...ouch! Players can also rip off some of your veggies from your garden and a single from your market stall, in fact most of the interaction in the game, which is almost non-existent, is from slight screwage cards (and the common pool draft). There might be enough to go around to balance out and our games were close, it looks like a pretty tight game where 4 players could all end with the same VP count and it comes to a money tie-break so I’m not sure how much of a factor this is yet. Apparently Uwe makes a suggestion to start with one regular customer and I think this might be a good idea (although this was for a "family version"? to make it easier but at least it will get you started and you'll know what to plant at the beginning for the first one)

Unless you just decide to all play simultaneous (after resolving the interaction/screwage cards) you are looking at some downtime here which causes it to drag. I think it might be a good idea to resolve the cards that matter and then everyone just go through their own actions, this would cut down some time otherwise you have nothing to plan for while you wait as well. Even still it might be one round or two rounds too long, similar to Le Havre for me. It might be best as 2 player just due to game length (although you could just play Jambo maybe....LOL) however tweaking the 3 or 4 player to be shorter is not something I'm afraid of... ;) My rating would be a little higher if game was shorter. 

Oh yes and the game is mostly cards and empty box, I would sure hope the English release will have a reduced price compared to the others in the trilogy everything considered. Rating will end between 7 and 8 for me most likely. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

2014-07-16

Initial Rating: 6.0 (April 2013)

I’m a huge Rosenberg fan. I still haven’t played a design from him I haven’t enjoyed. At the Gates of Loyang is not a bad game BUT it is perhaps one of the worst Rosenberg games I have tried, that’s not to say it is a bad game. Also I have to confess that I have only played it online so I need to play it face to face to see if that changes my mind about the game. When I do, I will come back and re comment.

For now I can say the theme seems very boring to me (Agricola´s and Le Havre´s theme is also boring but they are GREAT games) and, at first blush, it isn’t as deep as I’m accustomed from his games (but it retains its characteristically high playtime which is a minus here). Most of my games have been very close so I have felt the ending is a bit anticlimactic.

Bottom line, I need to play it again.

Current Rating: 6.0
[hr]
2014-10-06

I finally played the game face to face. It is a gorgeous game with all the vegetable bits. Also I have never played it with four players so the distribution phase was a mystery to me :D, now I understand.

It continues to be an OK game for me. This time I was able to buy a lot of VPs (unlike my online games), but still the game was very close so the anticlimactic feel I had before continues to appear. The issue here is that scoring more than 2 VPs in later stages can be very hard to accomplish.

In this game you feel you are playing by yourself (multiplayer solitaire), very low player interaction. I know there are some “attack” cards but the feeling persisted even after playing them. That would be ok if the game´s playtime wasn’t as long as it is. I’m also worried about the luck of the draw. I’m not sure if the luck is controllable or not. Sure, you can be creative when getting your vegetables (and it is fun to do this), but if other players have an easier time (less effort and resources) getting theirs, I feel they will win the game more often.

Bottom line, a good looking game, but it isn’t at the same level of Le Havre and Agricola (much lighter, increased luck) with similar play times. I need to try At the Gates of Loyang with 2 players face to face to see if it improves. I will keep in it in my game collection for now, but it has a high chance its rating will decrease.

Current Rating: 6.0

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm not sure what game I'd compare this to; certainly not the other members of the harvest trilogy.  I see how the vegetable planting is inspired by Antiquity but I'm not sure I see any other similarities other than the limited player interaction.  There are a lot of tradeoffs - steady income from regular customers vs. one-time bonuses from casual customers, early points (not the sucker bet they are in many resource management games) vs. infrastructure building.  Unsurprisingly for a Rosenberg title, the game feels very tight - not unlike Agricola where it seems like the most fun turn would be round 15, Loyang feels like it would be more fun with a few more options on each turn.

I appreciate that, for a resource management game, Loyang tries to break up the snowball by providing mild incentives to sink money into points early.

I think I'm playing this game exclusively 2-player unless all players are experienced.  The first game, a 4p learning affair, took over 3 hours which isn't acceptable.  Loyang wants to be finished in 90 minutes or less.

RATING: 7
This is the game that I wish Agricola could of been. Here you sell your vegatbles, since I don't eat any, what a deal. Great playing pieces. Nine rounds of brain thinking activity. Plant vegatbles, use helpers and feed regular customers and walk up casual customers. You are not alone, helpers come to assist you. The game drafting in the competive mode is a little dauting to casual gamers. The solo version is incredible. There is very little action with your opponents, so the competive game is almost like playing solitary. This is a game that I will play solo quite often. A smooth game. Helpers wording could of been explained better on the cards but thats what BGG is for. Easily one of the best solo games in the world. Enjoy.

RATING: 8
Opinions get skewed by comparison to Agricola and Havre; but judged in itself (as a medium-weight-puzzle-game) has a charm all its own.  Bits of St Petersburg, SJ, Antiquity, Agricola, Jambo, Bohnanza. Rated strictly as solo.  The solitaire rules eliminate most of the commonly cited defects (AP, too long, no interaction).  The "plant and harvest" mechanism is very sweet. The design overall is tight, with lots of self-regulating checks and balances. Rule book well written. Overpriced and boxed--both should be cut in half. The 2-3 player game introduces a card draft but it doesn't mix with the cerebral analysis/calculation the rest of game entails. So I think I'll stick to solo.  The 4p version seems artificial and weird. I wonder if it was designed solo then adapted? (See my [thread=587230]review[/thread].)

RATING: 8
Nice game that's a bit difficult to get into at first. The game is about playing cards to make money by taking best advantage of upcoming harvests of vegetables. You are trying to time the two aspects of the game to work together. More card-driven and chaotic than the other harvest trilogy games, this still quite fun trying to fit the pieces together. Play degrades with higher player counts because of a lack of interaction and increasing down-time between turns. However, with two players this shines. A good, strategic 2-player game that is not an abstract or a war game is hard to find. With Loyang, you've found it!

RATING: 8
Se parece al Agricola pero en mi opinión es bastante diferente.

Loyang es un juego en solitario, no tiene demasiada interacción entre jugadores. Durante la primera fase de cada ronda tus acciones pueden afectar a otro jugador al coger una carta del centro de la mesa, y durante la fase de acción puedes usar los tableros del resto de jugadores utilizando a tus ayudantes... y eso es todo. No hay pelea por colocarse en la mejor acción.

Por otro lado, en tu turno eres libre de hacer lo que quieras. Debes combinar tus acciones para poder optimizar tus recursos. Eso es una cosa que está bien ya que puedes desarrollar fácilmente tu estrategia pero hace muy difícil que los otros jugadores te puedan parar.

Como he dicho antes, bastante diferente del Agricola, pero es un buen rompecabezas.
[color=green]
LAST PLAY: 2015
[/color][color=blue]
It looks like Agricola but in my opinion it is quite different.

Loyang is a solo game, there is not so much interaction with the other players. During the first phase of a round your actions can affect another player by choosing a card from the pool, and during the action phase you can use the boards of the rest of the players by using your helpers... and that's all. No struggle for choosing the best action.

On the other hand, during your turn you are free to do whatever you want to do. You have to combine your actions in order to optimize your resources. That's good because you can easily develop your strategy but that's make quite difficult other player to stop you.

As I previously said, quite different game from Agricola, but a really nice puzzle to solve.
[/color]

RATING: 5
A game of extreme constraint in which you squeeze tiny advances from the system and spend the whole game in a tight pack with your opponents until one of you wins by a third of a point. Or, if you ignore the game's ridiculous (and nearly pointless) conversion of money to points, one of you wins by a handful of coins. [sarcasm]Let me tell you how excited this gets me.[/sarcasm]

The discussion of At the Gates on the forums has been quite insightful, so if you've read up on it you won't be surprised to hear that it's a meatier take on Jambo with Agricola's harvest mechanic. You manage a group of fields and a stable of customers in order to maintain something resembling an income. You use the income to purchase points, which gradually get more expensive every turn. On your turn you juggle a bunch of little calculations hoping to hit a burst of synergy from the cards, which, by the way, suck for you most of the time. The card drafting phase is all about finding the cards that don't completely suck at that particular moment. Let me tell you how... oh, wait.

RATING: 3
Quite a complex game, won't purchase but will play.

Don't EVER play this with anyone who suffers from AP!

This is essentially Jambo with a drafting mechanism for cards combined with a harvest supply mechanism for goods.

Not sure if I need a more complex version of Jambo.

I suffered some AP (First time ever for me) with this game 2 player (took 1:45), i can imagine that 4 players will be a nightmare. I can see that Uwe has tried to design around by 2 partners doing their actions together, but even so it seems inelegant.

Trying to work out the optimum trades and deliveries bearing in mind the Trader card etc. is asking for trouble.

Overall a decent gamers game, but one that requires more plays than i'll probably give it to unravel its full potential. Feels even more 'solitaire' than Agricola.

RATING: 6
A cute little game of farming vegetables and selling them at a profit. The core of the game is setting up an economic engine to expand your vegetable production to serve an ever increasing number of customers, while simultaneously siphoning off money into buying victory points (which you want to do early and do often due to some built in incentives). The economic system itself is very simple.

The main spice to the system comes from getting two cards a round into your tableau (e.g. different kinds of customers, special action cards). This is done with a simple drafting system. This is perhaps my biggest issue with the game. The cards are of very variable values, and since everyone always gets to keep one card of the 4 they buy, the random card draws each turn have a pretty big effect. It's very annoying to see others gaining extra fields while you're stuck with mediocre helpers, or those certain permanent customers you desperately need never coming from the very large draw deck that gets constantly shuffled.

All in all, I enjoyed Loyang, and expect to play it a lot more. It's no Agricola, but then few things are.

RATING: 8
It's hard to not make comparisons to Rosenberg's previous big box games, but after 8 plays, I feel like there's probably less to explore, but the big plus is that my wife really enjoys the game and asks to play it - she likes it more than the other two.

The theme and the wooden vegetable pieces are two of the best thing the game has going for it.  With experienced players (and especially with 2) the playing time is a lot more attractive than Le Havre, but I would still only want to play this with 2 or 3 (like Le Havre).

Some of the prevalent complaints don't bother me.  I can see how people don't like the scoring track.  It is easy to knock down your scoring marker, but you usually remember where you are.  It's true that most games will come down to the money tiebreaker, but you just have to see each coin as a victory point to get over that hump.  There is definitely a lot of luck in the card draw, but especially with 2 players I think there are a lot of cards to choose from and it is very unlikely that the available cards would hamper your game.

RATING: 7
Played that with a group of 4 at Spiel '09 on Friday afternoon. I like the game itself and the process of seeding, harvesting and card drafting & playing.

But I have one problem with Loyang at first. You can't plan ahead or a strategy and just have to make good tactical decisions during card drafting & action phase. So the downtime applies mainly to the dropped cards. I like downtime when I can make strategic decisions until it's my turn agin. But the downtime from Loyang was only tactical. 

Additionally I asked myself why the game takes 9 rounds and not 7 or 8. Might be interesting to ask beta testers, if they know the reason. I had the feeling that one could house-rule a round or two away.

I hope things speed up when all players are more experienced. Right now I'd prefer to play Agricola. But Loyang will be played again. I like it. Not more, not less.

Update:
The game seems to grow exceptionally after a second play. Therefore I bought it and eager to gain more experience :)

Update:
The solo version is awesome. I upped my rating by 0.5. Not ready to get a final conclusion for the 2-4 player version, but I'm more posivite compared to my Essen experience and will likely go up to 9 in a couple of weeks.

Update2:
It was not played a lot during the last year unfortunately. I would only consider to play it 1- or 2-player.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
One of Uwe Rosenberg's "Harvest Trilogy" games. To date, I haven't played a Uwe Rosenberg game that I disliked. This economical thinker has you place out varying cards with different abilities in each of 4 types. On your turn you can pick up a card from the offer and one from your hand to go out of the round. This to me has a similar feel to Zooloretto with less consequences for waiting too long, though you can be rewarded properly for pushing your luck. Otherwise, on your turn you are sowing vegetables/buying others/selling even more still and fulfilling customer demands and orders and using helpers to help you better accomplish this. After all of this happens you can spend your money to buy happiness (Victory Points). The game is intuitive and the working parts all mesh well together. The theme is decent. You can see your sown fields and your available vegetables to use. The components are brilliant. The box comes stocked with vegimeeples that add even more flash to the game! I would like to pick this one up to finish up my collection of The Harvest Trilogy.

RATING: 6
While this is likely my least favorite of the three latest offerings from Uwe, this one still has some interesting things going on within the game and it is beautifully produced!  Much of the game play is solo, however, with very little opportunity to really effect other people in their planting, harvesting, and selling of different veggies.  The game does intertwine some interesting aspects to the game with casual vs regular customers, special helpers that allow you to bend some of the rules and create a limited amount of player interaction, and markets at which you can trade one or two goods for some other good.  I think what I enjoy most about the game is that it is almost a puzzle which you are trying to solve - and it is very much about how to optimize what you make and how/when to convert your money into VPs.  

This game works very well as a solo game - largely due to the puzzle aspect.  I'm not sure how long this works until it gets repetitive - but it is a great way to experiment and try different strategies.

After further reflection, this game is likely best as a solo game and solid as a two player game. Don't bother with any other number.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
We find the game charmingly addictive.  The theme and components go a long way, but to be fair I think we enjoy cube pushers where you actually [i]do something[/i] with the cubes (we really disliked the arbitrary and abstract cube-pushing in [thing=24480][/thing] for that reason).  The planting, harvesting, and trading are just enough actions and theme for us.  The game has more interactivity than I think most people give it credit for---after just several games, we started watching each other during the card distribution phase.  And that doesn't count a well-timed Trader or Marketwoman that can devastate your opponent.  It's definitely a tactical optimization game, but after starting to learn the cards more, we're starting to be able to develop some longer term strategy.  On the surface, there is less tension than Agricola since there's no constant pressure of feeding your family.  But to play well, you have to create your own tension---you have to balance your tempo between the number of hungry customers and building up your farming engine, and going too far in one direction or another can leave you behind.  It's actually a deceptively tight game and a mistake in the beginning can cost you later on.

On the surface, it's somewhat like a deeper [thing=12002][/thing].  We do miss Jambo's richer, more diverse card set and I wish there were more cards here.  However, one of the things that Jambo lacks is that sense of building something.  [thing=39683][/thing] has that feeling of starting from nothing and building up a successful vegetable market.  By the time of the mid game, your engine should be set up and pouring out vegetables.  The end game does feel a little long, but it's just added pressure to keep adding fuel to your engine to keep it running.

RATING: 7
Part of Uwe Rosenberg's Harvest Trilogy.  "Agricola-Lite":  has a bit more randomness, requires a little more tactical decision making, and relies less on long-term planning.  While I prefer Agricola for its depth, "Loyang" offers a pleasant economic-type agricultural gaming experience.  The card phase draft-like mechanic is interesting, but can also really hurt some players if the cards they need just never come out.  The card phase also feels a little like "press your luck", as it is better to wait as long as possible in order to get the card you need, but at the expense of receiving a poorer spot in turn order.  TTFN

RATING: 8
Usually all players end at 17, 18 or 19 points, which can seem like its too close and samy-same every game. But remember that leftover coins count too, so your score is actually 18+X coins, like a decimal. And scoring 18+17 is a hell of a lot better than scoring 18+0.

The rules describe leftover coins as a tiebreaker, but its much more satisfying to disregard that, and read them into your score like above.

Apart from that, I like it a lot - I am after all a big fan of plowing fields in Agricola.
Also worth mentioning is the special "designers explanation of the rules" included - its a great way for experienced gamers to get into the game.

RATING: 8
Rating based on solo play only - I recently revisited Loyang, and was reminded how much I love the puzzle element and how you are forced to balance getting your vegetable engine going as efficiently as possible, and yet it means nothing if you don't put some of that hard earned efficiency towards your 'Path of Prosperity'. And that path is where the game comes to life for me, especially for solo play. You are trying to beat your previous score, but it means more than that. Points earned represent your business savvy and ultimate prestige and success within this bustling trading hub. It's hard work the vegetable business and it is reflected in not rewarding players with a squillion meaningless 'points' at the end of the game. 

That said, it's also refreshing to play a Rosenberg game that doesn't allow you to end the game with a negative score (I can handle it with the simple Patchwork, but it's hard to stomach with the punishing Agricola). I've never played it multiplayer but my instinct is that it shines solo; downtime could be an issue with others, and it's nice to avoid having to go through the, what seems, slightly unintuitive Distribution Phase, in place of a satisfying Grid alternative.

One and only blemish - I can see that the dummy player is functional but a little static and doesn't really replace a real person. There is an element of 'messing your opponents' plans up' in multiplayer that is missing in solo by stealing veg from their / your markets. 

The artwork is colourful and lovely to look at, and of course the wooden veggy-meeples are awesome! 

To broadly compare it to the other two early Rosenberg 'Harvest' games I would say Le Havre feels somewhat on-rails with the end game degenerating into a rush for the Shipping Line, Agricola feels punitive in the extreme (and a little like dumping a pile of hundreds-and-thousands all over your very bland bowl of pasta) and neither provide a solo experience that rivals Loyang.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a solid game, but it's far from Uwe Rosenberg's best. I find the game to be very tactical, with an emphasis on making good decisions from turn to turn, rather than working on an overall strategy. The game is a bit fiddly with many (albeit attractive) pieces. Perhaps the biggest downside to this game is turn length. In other games from this designer (Agricola, Le Havre) the player makes a single decision/action, and then the next player has a turn; in Loyang, each turn can potentially involve a dozen actions, which can induce analysis paralysis in even the most unthinking players. All that having been said, I do enjoy this game, and would gladly play it, though to someone not already familiar with Rosenberg's games, I'd recommend Le Havre or Agricola first.

RATING: 6
My first (and probably only) foray into the depths of vegetable trading was a four-player game, and it was surprisingly unfun. My general impression is that the original stuff works nicely, but is wrapped into an overly complex whole. I like how the dependable income from your regular customers is leavened by the need to fulfill their demands, and I love how the one-time shoppers are balanced by comparing their number to the regulars. I also like the trading phase and the opportunities it offers: smuggling a card that's more valuable for you than for others onto the market can be as satisfying as seeding the table with a valuable card to entice your opponents to exit early on.

However, the gameplay apart from the market phase is purely tactical, and mostly solitaire to boot. There is no satisfying narrative arc; you do the same optimisation puzzle every round, with a peak of vegetable income towards the late middle of the game. While this is probably a more honest depiction of the farming life than the steep progress in Agricola, one usually plays games as a respite from the daily drudgery, not as a repeat performance.

Furthermore, both multi-player options seem inherently flawed: I can't imagine the tedium of waiting for two other players in the three-player game, and the four-player game makes the inexcusably sloppy mistake of only giving three options for starting vegetables, arbitrarily putting two players in a worse spot for the market phase right from the start.

In summary, my rating of 6 might still be too charitable - I'll definitely never ever play this again with three or four, and I've got far too many wonderful other games for two players to make a Loyang duel a likely option.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Another terrific game from one of the best board game designers of all time.  The game just flows while the different mechanics seamlessly blend together. He proves once again that any theme (farming in ancient China) can be made into a game.

Like many of Uwe's games, this has a built in solo mode, and it is one of the most well regarded solos around for a reason. It has its own rules and presents a unique challenge for each game. A must for any solo gamer's library.

RATING: 8
This is my favourite solo game without a doubt. Viticulture with some Tusany elements is coming up a close second. 

I have never played a non solo game of this and am trying to work on a friend slowly so they give it a try ... hopefully they are not reading this!

Solo it is just a great peaceful puzzle that slowly develops in front of you. It is only after a couple of rounds that you start kicking yourself for planting the wrong vegetables in the wrong field a few seasons ago ... I mean how many other games can give you that feeling! Ok I will rephrase that. How many other non Uwe Rosenburg games can give you that feeling!

I managed 17 with 3 coins left over, which I thought was ok. I think this is an overlooked gem of a game, try it and let me know if you see things differently.

RATING: 9
At the Gates of Loyang is easy to play, but can be difficult to manage. First of all it is difficult in the first rounds of the game to build up your economy. The risk is that you take to much regular costumers or buy to many fields and then you run into debts and have to take a loan. This altogether makes it for me a good, but also a bit of a stressful game.

RATING: 9
Awesome 2p euro. One of the best components I've seen and nice artwork. It's the most accessible Uwe farming game. It doesn't have as much 'story' as Agricola, but enough - it's not yet another point salad - and the components look great. The cards get old pretty quick, but they add enough randomness to avoid mathing it out, and it's still very replayable as you can always try to improve your score. I've played this 10 times but my scores are still pretty bad. We do our turns simultaneously unless there's a specific card, then we deal with that in player order and continue. This way 2p games take about 45 minutes.

2-Player Comment:
* At the Gates of Loyang was designed for two. Best with two.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Aligning the theme of the market stall to turned based combo play is a subtle but brilliant move.  In Agric the game supports the mentality of "grow, expand, move ahead" with its dependance on actions as the primary resource.  Loyang on the other hand presents "opportunity" as its primary resource and by doing so shifts the mentality to "capitalize" where players are constantly looking for ways to leverage opportunity, either for direct gain or to set up increased opportunity in future turns.  Just like Agric, the game builds to a fever as the players are urged to take on more and more, again here in the form of opportunity, making those final turns tense, draining, but ultimately rewarding and beautiful.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
[microbadge=1714]

Thanks, Secret Soloist!

02-Jul-2015:  In Loyang, you are growing, selling, buying, and trading produce (vegetables).  The solo game is all about the balance between efficiency and long term planning.  There is a tableau of cards from which the player may purchase customers, helpers, and/or new fields.  This is the heart of the solo game as the market will change each round bringing in new opportunities for the player to succeed in his plans.

The game is ultimately a high score affair, which turns many solo gamers off.  However, in Loyang, every VP is a struggle, an achievement.  At the end of your turn, you may pay one coin to move ahead one space on the VP track.  If you want to continue moving on the track that same turn, you must pay for that space in the same number of coins as VPs.  It's ingenious.  And brutal.  And frustrating.  And so satisfying when you can reach those higher 17 and 18 point levels.

The components are lovely in this one as well.  All of the vegetables are brightly colored, appropriately shaped wood pieces.  The cards are decent quality with nice artwork.  The overall presentation is very nice.  I did have trouble grasping the game from reading the rulebook, but after watching a video or two, I was good to go.  I don't think the rulebook is bad, it just doesn't convey what's happening on the table as clearly as I think it should.

Either way, an excellent addition to my solo game collection.  It's one I want to bring out more often in the near future.  Great stuff.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
At the Gates of Loyang has many unique ideas. I really like how the scoring works but fear that it may be very difficult to catch up if you're behind. It's already a heavy tactical game with only a bit of interaction so in some cases players will just be trying to beat their own scores, like in a golf match. 

I've only played this against opponents online and it's very mathy. I fear that in person all of the calculations would just bore the heck out of players awaiting there turn. Decisions are challenging as every dollar counts. It's not a loosey goosey at all. Extra money is particularly difficult to come by since one needs more each round in order to move up the VP track.

RATING: 9
Loyang is related to but much different than Le Havre and Agricola. It is not as tense or agonizing. Indeed, it is actually relaxing to play as you puzzle through the various combinations and equivalencies of the trading options. The card play keeps the game moving seamlessly. You always have something to think about and there is no down time. The colorful components are pleasing and beckoning.

I have only played this 2 player and I find it thoughtful and calculating without being brain-busting or thick. Yet it requires careful thought and planning, and some subtle strategy as you maximize your options. 

Wonderful game and great for 2.

RATING: 7
My favorite Uwe Rosenburg design to date. The game starts off a little tense as you try to get your engine off the ground, but it becomes more and more of a relaxing, procedural experience as the game goes along. The game has such a nice ebb and flow to it. It's almost relaxing and a little brain-burning at the same time, which is a notable achievement of this game. Some of the wording on the cards is a little awkward, and the game doesn't reward much in the way of variation, but I really enjoy my plays of this game. Even if it does have that quirky Rosenburg feel to it, where the game feels a little scatter-brained on what your focus should be.

RATING: 6.5
EDIT 2015-05-31: Note de 7.2 (mai 2013) revue à la baisse. Aucune envie d'y jouer. Trop procédural, trop influencé par le hasard des cartes accessibles et obtenues via les "two-pack". Le jeu semble nous jouer, et je ressens plus de stress et de maux de tête que de plaisir en y jouant.


**

À deux joueurs, un certain élément "chance" dans l'obtention de cartes clés peut altérer la suite du jeu. 

Sur une note plus positive, j'apprécie particulièrement la mécanique de draft (Card phase) qui se répète neuf fois durant la partie (tout le contraire d'Agricola!). Nous sommes constamment confrontés à des dilemmes, et il faut savoir planifier et bien lire le jeu pour y connaître du succès. 

Techniquement, la phase d'actions laisse aux joueurs une très grande liberté, mais, bien souvent, l'environnement austère dans lequel oeuvrent les joueurs condamne ces derniers à exécuter ce qui peut et doit être exécuté.

Un jeu où il faut donc maximiser la rentabilité de chaque décision. Très calculatoire, peut-être trop à mon goût.

À mon goût, je préfère ce jeu à Agricola, mais je sais qu'il sera un jour dans ma liste de jeux à échanger.

Note: Le matériel est agréable à manipuler, mais peut faire perdre beaucoup de temps. Aidez vos adversaires pour économiser du temps, et laissez-les vous aider à votre tour!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game completes the 'harvest trilogy', but was designed before Agricola. In ancient China, in the card phase, one has to choose which card to keep and which one to make available to opponents. Then during the action phase, you plant vegetables, then sells them to customers, buy a 2 pack of cards, play helpers, buy vegetables, etc. You are trying to make the most money so you can advance on the path of prosperity. The first step always cost you $1, but the next steps cost the value.

This game works well with 2 players and clocks in at two hours or so. Unfortunately, with more players, it takes a very long time and bogs down. I would only play this with 2 players, and it is a good fun game, with tactics and trying to improvise. What prevents this from being a top tier game is some of the helpers can interfere with your opponents, and you have to wait for your opponent during their action phase. Having said that, I do enjoy this game, and the implementation on yucata.de is fun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Edit:  first solo play tonight.  Fantastic!  

Edit:  This is one of my daughter's favourite games, and so most of my plays are two-player against her.  Love it at two players.

RATING: 6.5
Not quite sure what to make of this unusual puzzle game. On the plus side, it feels complete. Lots to do on each turn, and the game builds turn by turn, opening up more options, increasing income, increasing freedom of action. On the negative side, planning-luck in the early game can swing the game. Like, if you have an extra radish, and on a whim you decide to sow it, and then the next turn you happen to draw a customer who requires radishes -- it's planning, but planning for something that is a blind luck draw. Same with luck of the draw in general, as you could potentially be shut out of the lucrative regular customer market for several turns. Finally, I found it incredibly difficult to grasp the game from the rulebook, even though it was written and organized in the same style as Agricola, Le Havre, and Ora, all of which are more complex games yet have more easily grasped rules. Perhaps this game is less intuitively organic than those (i.e., it's rulesy)

RATING: 8
I have mostly played this solitaire.

This is an enjoyable economic puzzle.  The brain burn seems a little more pure somehow than that of Le Havre and Agricola.  It is probably the simplicity - grow vegetables, trade 'em, and, more importantly, sell 'em!  This is a very nice short (hour or less) solo puzzle.  I have played this with another person once.  The interaction is subtle, much less than the other two.

This type of game causes me angst in the rating system.  It is a really smooth, well put together game.  So it should be highly rated.  However, using the BGG scale, it is not a game I would suggest playing.

Why this is not a 10:
-> I do not want to play it all the time
-> Of all the solitaire-like games, this one really does feel that way; If I have an opportunity to play someone, I will pick a more interactive game

Why I still give it an 8:
+ It is an excellently designed game
+ It is perfect for solitaire play
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Brilliant game, especially for 2 players. 

I don't agree with those who say it's a solitaire game. There's a bit of interaction, enough to spoil rival player's turns from time to time. I played some 2 player games in yucata with plenty of interaction that decided the outcome. 

The issue is: usually you can annoy one rival at the cost of a minor loss to you. In a 2 players game, the loss of your rival is your win, but in a 3-4 players game, the other players are "free riders that benefit from your confrontations; thus, the game is more prone to become a "solitaire".

Likes:
-> Plenty of choices/paths
-> Above average strategy
-> the drafting mechanism (very clever!) reduces the randomness of the cards
-> Perfect mix of interaction for me (not possible to gangbang the leader, can annoy rivals at a minor cost for you)
-> The richer doesn't get richer
-> Great components
-> Good replayability

My negative point: prone to AP.

RATING: 8
After his two hitherto biggest smashes (Bohnanza and Agricola) both dealt with veg, Uwe Rosenberg resorted again to farming for Vor den Toren von Loyang. 

Only slightly different in topic to both aforementioned games (this time it's production and sales of veggies) and more or less between them in terms of difficulty, Vor den Toren vor Loyang offers a near-solitaire gaming experience with a considerable modicum of luck (the game relies heavily on drawing cards... many of which can't be saved for later). Notwithstanding, players have lots of room to plan which veg they're going to stick to in order to build a quick and efficient production line, and also what sorts of clients (regular or occasional) they want to sell their veg to.

Even though the packaging might suggest Vor den Toren von Loyang continues the streak of heavy Rosenberg games started with Agricola, it's ultimately a different beast: a lighter, quicker, more agile kind of game that doesn't make as much impact but can be played a couple of times in a row.

RATING: 10
[b]At the Gates of Loyang[/b] was received in a trade (4/30/14)

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (10):[/COLOR]

[i]I still love this game, even if it hasn't been easy to get it to the table. This seems to be the most overlooked of Uwe's big farm games and I'm not sure why. I find that it really captures the theme of running a market stall all the way from planting crops, to managing inventory and keeping customers happy. I love figuring out the logistics puzzle I am presented wit each time I play. Yo uare always presented with different challenges because you never know what customers are going to want, and it is not always easy/cheap to row the crops you need.

I want to briefly touch on the scoring track in the game which only goes to 20. While that may seem like a very constrained scoring track the tie breaker is money, which are basically additional points. Not only that, the amount of planning it takes to get your score from 16 to 17 to 18 is unbelievable. This is not an easy game, improving your score by 1 point after multiple plays felt like quite an accomplishment. I'm continually hovering at 17 with one 18 point game under my belt. I can't imagine scoring 19 or 20.

This also happens to be the first game I recently played solo. I really enjoyed it, although it is a lot to keep track of without having another player who is helping you. I plan on trying it again solo and my son will play with me again, I just wish I could find an opponent who enjoys the game as much as I do.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (9):[/COLOR]

[i]- Great theme, with beautiful production from the artwork to all the wooden vegimeeples.
- Another Uwe game that plays awesome with 2 and doesn't involve feeding anyone.
- The card drafting system is really tense because you are discarding cards to a common pool that both players can choose from. However; the twist is that you must choose a card from the common pool and your hand.
- There is something therapeutic about planting fields of vegetables and then harvesting them. It is a tense game, but somehow I stay very relaxed while playing.
- Great variety of card types (long term customers, one time customers, market stands and helpers) and character abilties on the helper cards.
- Figuring out how to optimally fulfill orders to customers while also making sure you are going to continue to harvest the vegetables you need is a great, brain burning puzzle you have to figure out each turn.

I really am impressed with this game, I can't believe a game with a 20 point score track seems to have lots of replay ability. I was initially nervous that the game could be solved, but it is impossible because you never know which cards you will get, and which order your fields come out in. 
[/i]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I'm a fan of low interaction games where you have decent control over your own destiny, but I'm wondering if this hugely tactical beast has too little interaction even for me. Each turn you're trying to maximise your money to advance multiple steps along the VP track. Your opportunities to make money each round will be dictated by the 2 cards you'll keep (via the novel discard / draft mechanism), plus turning the vegetables you've laid in previous turns into money this turn the best way you can, done via Jambo style demand cards. There are a bunch of "helper" cards which allow you to use other players things, but I was surprised how little that affected the play (although I'm sure some cards will cruel an opponent at times). The score track was a good feature - pay $1 for the first VP you acquire each turn, and also $x for each additional space you'd like, where x is the number of VPs you'll get to as a result of each move. It provides some interesting dilemmas in the early to mid-game where you're weighing up investing the money into your money-producing engine for later VPs vs acquiring cheap VPs now that may win you the game. The engine building / VP generating challenge was interesting in and of itself, with lots of ways to combine your actions (resulting in serious downtime at times however), and probably worth a higher rating, but I mark it down in the end due to the lack of tension induced by its solo nature, the impression it leaves that each game will play out exactly the same as the first and that your success will be dependent on whether cards come along that match your production at the times you need them.

RATING: 7.5
Based on one play: As with Uwe's other games, there is a lot of bits and movement of resources, but other then that, it is very different. You play two cards a turn and choose another player (in a four player game) that you can interact with. You try and produce as much as possible to fill orders and then deliver them to get money. The money is used to buy points at the end of each round. Of the cards you play one comes from your hand and one from the previously discarded cards of others, so you must time your selection to get the cards you want and the partner you want. Quite original mechanics and you can see the progression, but watch out for your fields as once they are empty, you lose them. Definitely want to play again.

RATING: 8
I really love the individual playing boards and the way this games flows and ties together. Although not my favorite game, I'll still play this almost any time someone asks. It has a very different style of play, in that the most important thing is a good plan/strategy that has to flow from turn to turn. What you plant now has to produce everything you need for several turns, but the market stalls can help you if you just need one of something, but timing is so important to get things planted so you can harvest them when you need them. It's so hard to explain how this game flows, but when you play it, it is so engrossing and because of the staff (people cards) - you have to constantly be aware of what other players have and what they are doing, so you can save what you need and gain from other players when you have a good staff card. Very involving game that never plays the same way twice.

RATING: 6
[b]Comment:[/b] Solid Uwe resource management game. You run a farm and a vegetable stand and need to sell specific combinations of vegetables to different customers. You can see some Agricola in it but it's is pretty different and stands on it's own well. The different vegetable meeples are really cute. Everything in the game is built on a card distribution mechanic, where each player gets a few cards in their hand, and they either add a card to the central pool, or take a card from the pool and one from their hand and put the rest into the pool for other players. You need to pay attention to what other people need and try not to feed them. It adds a nice card drafty tension.

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:nostar: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:nostar: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[b]RO[/b]: [i]7/21/2017[/i]
At The Gates of Loyang (or simply "Gates" as we refer to it) is another lovely agricultural economic in Uwe Rosenburg's rather large catalogue of agricultural economics. It is a tight game of raising various vegetables (represented by those chunky, charming vegimeeples) and satisfying your customers. Along the way you'll hire helpers and contract suppliers with which you can trade vegetables you have for the one you need.

Likely one of the most unique (and also my least favorite) mechanic is the scoring mechanism. Every turn you can pay one "cash" (the game's currency) to advance one spot on the track, regardless of where you are on it. Then, you may pay the amount that the subsequent space(s) are worth to continue advancing until you run out of cash or choose to stop. (For example, you begin your turn on number nine on the score track. You may pay one cash to advance to 10 on the score track. If you wish to advance to 11 that turn you must pay 11 cash, and then pay 12 more if you wish to advance again).

That minor caveat aside Gates is an outstanding game that I would highly recommend to fans of Uwe's designs, nice components, and quirky themes. Also of note most people seem to think three is the best player count for this and that may be, but Gates plays great with two.

RATING: 7
Loyang was not what I was expecting.  It's a card game.  (Who could tell from the gigantic box?)  Since I like card games, I actually like Loyang.  The thing that stood out for me was the way that the theme really seemed reflected in the mechanics.  While there was a lot of rearranging of bits which made it seem fiddly, the moving around of stuff made me feel like I was doing a lot of trading and running around--even if I was only trading stuff within my own play area.

But with four players, it's annoying. I still am not sure if we were supposed to be doing everything simultaneously or not during the pick a partner phase.  Plus, in almost all situations, it didn't really matter who my partner was.

But I liked it.  The card and hand management was all there. The veggie bits were adorable.  It's a solid two  player game--one that I wouldn't be sorry to play again.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is probably my favorite Rosenberg game so far. I like the flow of the game phases better here than Agricola. The game can be a little fiddly with a ton of moving parts, quickly filling the table with cards. There's a lot to consider each turn which can result in some serious downtime.
I think the card drafting phase really shines here, where there were many times I felt in control of who would become first player for actions (an often important position to be in!)
Most actions are meaningful and result in a tight race for VP. Many different paths to victory. I like the "two-pack" action, but feel it adds some unnecessary luck to the game.
Overall: A very solid game all about balance. Playing it safe will leave you left behind but taking chances can leave you very sore. Looking forward to looking at this one from more angles.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
At the Gates of Loyang is a very typical Rosenberg game. You start with almost nothing and then produce and convert resources (vegetables). AtGoL is also more straight-forward than the other Rosenberg Games. Instead of building buildings and producing all kind of other goods or feed your family as side job, here you focus just on growing and selling vegetables (which then roughly equates to victory points). I really like the concept of permanent costumers, which give you kind of the same feeling as feeding your family in Agricola (but as mentioned is not a "side quest"). Playing with vegemeeples makes running the game more easily (compared to chits in O&L) and increases the appeal of the game. 
Initial Rating: 7

[i]Update[/i]: On second play the extreme multiplayer-solitaire nature of the game became really apparent. Because of the almost absent interaction AtGoL is more puzzle than game (much more than other Rosenberg titles). Rating decreased to 6.5

RATING: 9.5
Plays very well with two. Sow and harvest vegetables, and select and use action cards to serve regular and casual customers for cash. Interesting card selection mechanism (one from the courtyard and one from your hand) and scoring (first step costs 1, each subsequent step costs the number on the step). Seems like replayability will be great with all the action cards, which add abilities, opportunities, and twists (trading at market stalls, buying goods, stealing goods, buying fields, gaining customers). Gorgeous artwork and solid mechanics make a tight, fun game. Loans can easily lose you the game.

2010-12-26: I've realized I'm not so into these kinds of fiddly games with tons of different cards with unique effects all in play at the same time. Complexity from a complex system with lots of stuff is less interesting to me than complexity from a simple elegant system that has deep consequences.

RATING: 7.5
Have very much enjoyed my couple of plays and want to continue to explore in 2018. Loyang has what I might call a 'sharp sloppy' design, meaning that there's continual randomness to be handled which can provide uneven opportunities (that's the sloppy), but constant opportunity & necessity for the player to handle and ride whatever providence hands them in order to compensate correctly. The semi-random opportunities arrive on a conveyor belt which makes multiple passes throughout the game, so it seems perfectly reasonable to me so far that the balance may be tuned to a fine degree. Time will tell. Regardless: no question that I've enjoyed this one so far. I just have to watch out for its AP-inducing nature, because much like its other cousins by Uwe, each turn is potentially fully calculable.

RATING: 7
Rating after 1 play. 

Not sure what to think about this game  yet, although I did have a good time while playing it. It was a two player game with my gf, although it's basically multiplayer solitaire so I doubt if adding players would really make a big dif.  

There are 3 phases to the game, in the first one you simply take one good of each field you have and then draw a new field. The next phase is the card phase, in which you get 4 new cards, but can only keep 2. Sorta. This phase seemed unnecessarily complicated. Why could't we just draw 4 cards in the beginning and 2 each in all the following phases? Why all the complicated, game lengthening bs? Why Uwe, why?! Anyways, the card phase as it is turned me off each round, resulting in me not really wanting to continue. But once it was over and we moved on I started enjoying myself again. The next phase is when you do stuff. There is a list of actions you can take, any number of times and in any order, except 1 can only be done once. Kinda like Reef Encounter. Except in this game you are trading goods, mostly with "Market Stall" cards that you drew (or acquired) in the card phase, and then delivering them to "Customer" cards that you got in the card phase. This can make for some pretty sweet combos and a nice satisfying smugness for pulling off something widely complex.  At the end of the round each player buys VP's.

I'll be giving this one another shot for sure, as it's thinkyness level is just about right for me, but the card draw may give it a level of randomness that just isn't acceptable in this kind of game. Time will tell!

RATING: 5
A good economic engine and logistics game that will probably play more or less the same with each outing once you've solved the basics. That's not a terrible thing because it's the fun bit discovering what works and what works better. It's more tactics than strategy. While it doesn't have the depth of Le Havre, of the trilogy games it's the easiest to pick up and decent to pull out once in a while.

One negative issue with the game is the downtime caused by the not overly significant player interaction. The character cards often mean players must wait for others before performing their actions. If you want to get around this you can ask if another player is going to do anything to you and then play on if they're not. However, with the purchasing of new cards each turn possible you feel guilty about stuffing up the flow of things by playing a newly acquired character card. It's not a big thing, but it certainly is one reason I think this game maxes out at three players.

I do like the card selection mechanic. Mostly you're playing for turn order than any magic card appearing, but it's nice when a terrific card appears anyway. It's a cleverly simple system that works very well.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The best of Uwe's big box games, surprisingly it isn't the one that gets the best ratings. Perhaps the success of his previous designs left this game without a space in the ludic world. At the end, it is about getting resources in order to score points, as most Euros, but the game changes so much that it is not as easy as building a machine and let it work, having the players to constantly reinvent themselves instead.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
As my friend put it after our game, Uwe seems to design solitaire exercises, then throws in a little (sometimes false) interaction with some texty cards... 

Agricola was highly interactive because each worker placement space was limited to 1 placement, and that was really a good game. I didn't play Le Havre, but it didn't look better to me - in fact, it looked worse. I'm not even sure I can identify why exactly.

Loyang is fairly obviously a solitaire game with interaction tacked on. It seems to me to be a game designed for players who play games "for fun" and don't try to win. To me that doesn't make sense - the point of a game is to win, so if you're not going to try to win, then why start the game in the first place?

Played like a solitaire game, largely ignoring what other players do and just doing your own thing, and at the end you see if you happened to win or not, I could see the game going faster. But if you try to win, you constantly have to track who has what helper (in case they could trade our resource out from under you), who has what market stall (in case you want to swap with them), etc. Therefore it takes much longer than it should, and a lot more work than it should, for something which is 90% bookkeeping.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
[hr][microbadge=1714][listitem=2847386]At the Gates of Loyang - Solitaire Tracker Geeklist Item[/listitem]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
At the Gates of Loyang is essentially a logistical puzzle of getting the right veggies to customers on time. The major choke point is that each field only produces one vegetable each round, so players have to plan ahead to ensure a steady supply of vegetables. The forward planning aspect also makes the seemingly minor disruptions by opponents' Helpers actually matter a lot.

Despite the lack of flashy mechanisms or complex networks, Loyang is my favourite among Rosenberg's big box efforts. This is a streamlined game with clearer tradeoffs, and it strikes an ideal balance between long-term planning and tactical drafting decisions. I also appreciate that it requires constant thinking, but without frying my mental circuits every now and then like [thing=35677][/thing] does. The only major caveat is that luck of the draw is felt more keenly with a restrictive scoring system. (A minor caveat would be that the artwork leaves me feeling like I'm interacting with the cast of Mulan.)

As a side note, I evaluate this largely as a 2P game. Adding a third player adds to game length without introducing appreciable value otherwise. On the other hand, the solo game is actually quite good, as the card grid introduces an aspect of manipulating its movement and adds to the foresight of upcoming cards.

RATING: 9
#40 on my Top 100 Games of All Time (2017)
#23 on my Top 100 Games of All Time (2016)
#54 on my Top 100 Games of All Time (2015)

I only learned Loyang recently after owning it for years. The joke is on me because this game is fantastic! You're trying to make the most profitable farm. You have to pay gold to advance on the path of prosperity (the victory point track). You can buy as many as you'd like at the end of each of the nine rounds. The first one costs 1 gold and the next costs a number of gold equal to its value. So if you're on space 4, it would cost 1 gold to move up to 5, but then 6 more gold to move up to 6 and so on. Thus, it's easier to advance more than one space a turn at the start of the game. However, you're super poor at the beginning as you haven't had time to build up an engine. Just another stellar design by Uwe and one I look forward to playing much more!

RATING: 5
It's a good game that works well. Clearly this was developed from Bohnanza and was the inspiration for Agricola.  Nice ideas, but I feel that it suffers from too many serious issues.  

First and foremost, the downtime is brutal.  You must go in turn order as minor things can change by the time it is your turn.  Some of the cards interact so turn order is important. So that means you have the long wait for your turn to roll up.  When it is your turn, there is so much to do that even if you are actively moving all of your crap around, you can't help but feel guilty by holding everyone up (despite the fact that they'd all held you up).  It is an efficiency model, so it is important to make sure that everything is in place.  If you do things out of order it costs you more, so even if you know exactly what needs to be done it takes time to figure out the optimal way to do it.  God forbid you play this with someone with analysis paralysis.

Second, it is overly long and repetitive.  It is kind of interesting in the beginning, but then you find that you are just doing the same kind of stuff over and over again. An hour later you are still doing the exact same thing only now you are collecting different suits.  It isn't a short game, and it has no story arc, so the repetition really is exacerbated.

Third, the luck of the draw is too extreme. If cards come your way you are stoked, if they don't it forces a little more creativity, but really if you are only drawing trading merchants with your two pack, aka Tupac, you are going to be really bummed.

Fourth, there never is an exciting moment in the game.  Even when people gang up on you and give you a good humping, the result is usually that you have to pay an extra couple dollars to do something close to what you wanted to do.  Now I'm not a fan of "take that" mechanisms, here it seems like it is a desperate attempt to force interaction in the system.  In concept, I'm glad that the attempt was made, but the result compounds the need for turn order and thus the downtime.  So while I'm pleased that the way cards hit you it isn't too extreme, they don't really matter all that much.  So you just kind of muddle your way through the endless motion of moving cubes.

Perhaps I'd like this game more as some online implemented game where you just check in once a day, but as is, it just isn't what I want in a face to face board game.

RATING: 6.4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Upfront I'll state that this is my least favorite game of the Big Box games I've tried by Uwe Rosenberg, but by no means is it a bad game. For me, a 6 is still above average (although not by much) and I'll play the game from time to time. 
Like other Uwe Rosenberg games this is a resource management and optimization game, but more so than the others this is a logistical puzzle. You have to manage up to 9 fields where you can harvest up to 6 different types of vegetables, ultimately to satisfy both your regular and your incidental customers. Sell your vegetables with the most profit to buy the most victory points and win the game.
Unlike Agricola there is no worker placement mechanic and unlike Le Havre and Ora et Labora there is no common pool of buildings to buy and use. Because of this, this game lacks most of the indirect interaction the other games have to offer. The player interaction comes in the form of a card drafting round at the start of each turn and cards come in 5 varieties: extra fields, regular customers, incidental costumers, markets (to trade vegetables) and helpers for all kinds of benefits.
My two biggest gripes with the game are not being able to plan your actions more than the current turn which doesn't relly fit well with a resource management logistical puzzle in my opinion. The second gripe is that in a game lacking most player interaction, some of the helper cards that do let players interact are pretty harmful. As it is, I'd prefer the game to be a strictly multiplayer solitaire engine game. That's probably better than a game where I can't interact with you, but you can interact with me because you drew the right cards. As a result of both these gripes and the fact that players can do a lot of stuff on their turn downtime is an issue, even more so as you can't really plan ahead during other players turns.
Also, I've never played with more than two players, but there are some very clunky three and four player rules.
Still, by no means is this a bad game, but just not a favorite of mine.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Update after several more plays at Yucata: the game is [b]much[/b] better with just 2 players! 

---
An interesting game, but after 2 plays I think I still don't really get it. At least, I don't get how to do [i]well[/i] at it! There are some really interesting mechanisms here: simultaneous turns (4 players), and the card exchange (pretty brilliant, really!). But there seems to be somewhat significant randomness and chaos here, too, mostly due to the way the cards come out (your own fields, but mostly from the deck of cards): access to extra fields, getting the "right" customers, luck of the two-pack draw, and the occasional player interaction where one person has a helper who can steal your market stall or customer or helper! The game is almost multiplayer solitaire -- which would be fine since I generally enjoy building an efficient engine, except for those semi-random (screw-your-partner) cards!
And as others have noted, the game seems to go on a bit too long. 

I'll stick with Le Havre as my favorite so far!

--- Summary ---
Players each have a deck of fields (2 of each card, each shuffled into top/bottom half) and a starting field, with 3-9 spaces on them. They also have a personal market from which to buy/sell veggies; start the game by buying and planting one veggie -- when you plant, fill the other spaces of the field with the same veggie (from Supply). In future turns, you'll harvest 1 veggie/field (empty field is discarded). After harvesting, deal out 4 cards/player. In turn, players either discard a card into the face up pool, or play ONE card from their hand and ONE from the pool (then discard the rest into the pool). The last player to play cards picks the 1st or 2nd player to play as their partner (4er game). Then, the 3rd and 4th players simultaneously take their turns, but any interaction cards may only be played on their "partner". After that, the other 2 players play.

On their turns, players will be buying/selling veggies to the market or customers (cards), planting veggies into fields, etc. The variability here comes from the combination of cards you've managed to acquire: regular customers demand the same veggies x 4 turns (steady income) but cost you money if you fail to deliver, and they have lower payoffs than casual customers (who become available randomly from the deck of cards). At the end of each player's turn, they may buy 1 VP for $1, and additional VP for the cost shown on the next space of the VP track (1->20). 

The game ends after 9 rounds; the player with the most VP wins (tiebreaker = cash)!

RATING: 6
I got this game as a collector and huge fan of Uwe Rosenberg's other "harvest trilogy" games (Agricola, Le Havre, and later Ora et Labora).

All three of those games feel very similar and I love them all but this one doesn't feel the same.  The game is played by drafting cards that give you fields, customers, market stalls, and helpers that allow you to harvest and sell crops for money.  You buy your victory points each turn and they are cheaper the earlier in the game you buy them.

Each player ends up with a miniature economy and the money is very hard to come by.  This is a fine game and the balance is commendable but it doesn't interest me as much as Rosenberg's other efforts.  Aside from some of the helper cards there is virtually no interaction and the game ends up really just about creating the most efficient personal economy.  This gets a bit dry.

It is still a decent game and one I don't mind playing but also not one I'm going to bring out on my own too often.  I've heard this game described as Antiquity: the Card Game and I definitely get that with the harvest mechanics and the complex puzzle feel.

RATING: 7
A challenging economic game, and exactly what one can expect, given that this is the predecessor of Agricola.  There is a little more of a luck component to this game, as the cards one draws affects what options the players have, and although there is a card exchange, it is typically filled with the cards one wants the least.  We wound up seeding it with one random card in our two-player game in order to give more selection, since the blind-draw from the deck can result in a customer that could be an ongoing points penalty.  I do like that the catch-up mechanic has to do with buying your way forward, so the further ahead you are, the more it costs to maintain that advantage.

RATING: 7
Producing goods, selling to customers, then buying VP. While Le Havre felt like Agricola (but different) this actually felt more of a descendent of Antiquity (an acknowledged influence on Agricola) since your 'income' is basically goods harvested each round. Loyang has no worker placement. What it does have is a number of interesting mechanics that seem novel variants on familiar mechanics, but they combine to create a tight game of developing goods-generating streams, drafting cards to create trade/special action/customer abilities, then striving to satisfy the demand you've created to earn cash which you have to convert into VP on an ongoing basis.

RATING: 5.75
Love the pieces and the art! However, the gameplay is a different matter. Don't get me wrong, the gameplay is good -- but there are some problems. The game suffers from a run-away leader problem that is mainly determined in the first 3 rounds of the game. There is a high luck factor in those turns, and getting the right Regular Customer can pretty much ensure victory over anyone else because it is nearly impossible to overtake someone on the Path to Prosperity unless they mess up completely (not likely to happen with any experienced players) or get REALLY bad luck in their cards (again, not that likely).

Of course, this was my impressions from my first play of the game -- I'll have to play it again to really get a good feel for it. I like it, but I hate it. It needs SOMETHING to make it work better, not sure what that something is right now, but hopefully another play or two will help me figure it out.

WARNING: This game can get very long in analysis paralysis! Our first game (a 3-player game with rules and some minor examples before we started) lasted nearly 4 hours.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Original (05-22-17): My favorite game! There's no real diversity in scoring or strategies, it's just figuring out the best way to maximize profit from round to round. However, there's just something about how all the parts integrate together and make such a wonderful game.

There's the tension created by the card distribution phase, where players have to potentially risk cards they need by putting them up for everyone else to have a chance to take before they can play it. There's the brain-straining contemplation from figuring out the best combination of actions that will squeeze out that extra cash. Then having to decide how to spend your hard earned money, either advancing along the scoring path or investing in things to make future profit.

The game includes a rather well designed solo game as well, which is pretty fun to play. Not as good as the multiplayer game but still helps to make the game worth buying even if it's hard to get to the table with others.

Overall, a very well designed game that continues to remain challenging even after several plays. 10/10

RATING: 5.5
I really like the mechanics involved in this game, but when it comes to actually playing, the different mechanics get in each others way.

This review is based on the 3-payer game, which the rules seem to encourage as the intended way to play the game.

The semi-drafting of cards means that newer players can unknowingly give more experienced players insane cards for fear that the mediocre card they want gets taken (but that's fine by itself).

What really hurts the game is the fact that it is impossible for players to take their turns at the same time because of the red worker cards. Even if there are no interactive elements on the board, players can luck into a relevant worker with the "Buy a Two-Pack" action that it seems a waste not to use almost every turn.

So players must wait for every other player's turn, casing a significant amount of downtime where there probably isn't anything important going on, but there [i]could[/i] be. The downtime in this game is killer.

I once had a friend pull out a newspaper while playing a game, and I never thought I would find a game that warrants that kind of treatment until now.

RATING: 6
I'll start off by saying I played this 3 player when, in retrospect, this should really only be played 2 player.  There is A LOT of downtime in the game, and it's the bad kind of downtime where you are twiddling your fingers most of the time instead of deeply engrossed in what your opponents are doing.  I think it would be OK 2 player, but with 3 player it took way too long.  I think our game was close to 3 hours, but it feels like a game that should be 60-90 minutes.

The game play mechanisms are fairly standing farming tropes, but I have to give the game credit for the card play mechanism.  At the start of each round, each player is dealt a hand of 4 cards.  Then, players take turns doing one of two things.  The first option is to discard one card from your hand into a community pool of cards.  Or, you can choose one card from your hand and one card from the community pool and those will be your two cards for the round.  If you do this, the rest of the cards in your hand get added to the community pool.  If you wait until you only have one card left, you have to take the second option.  This was a really neat twist on drafting that I would like to see in more games.

After one play I'm pretty sure I would rather play [thing=31260][/thing] every day of the week, but I would like to try this with 2 players and see if it shines.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Totally worth my time and will always be happy to play :) The randomness of the cards makes the game replayable, and there are SO MANY combinations and situations that come up, which is why it's a 9 and not a 10.
