RATING: 5
This is an initial rating ... I have hopes that trying Loyang as a two-player game will improve my opinion of it. However, I'm unlikely to play this again with four players, as it becomes a massive time-suck, far exceeding the advertised 100 minutes. I like some of what's going on here, and the choice of whether to buy cheap, early victory points or to plow money into an engine that pays off in the later stages is difficult (and balanced). The veggie-meeples are cute, and there's a lot of room for tactical creativity by players. All in all, a decent game that I fear is a bit (and possibly quite a bit) too long for what it is, and certainly not rising to the rarefied air that Uwe's masterpiece Agricola (or even Le Havre) inhabits. Update: I have now tried this with two. Definitely better than with three or four, but still too long. The bits are great and it's a harmless way to pass the time, but its a puzzle game with little interaction, the downtime is too long (never mind the overall game length) and the decisions just aren't all that interesting.

RATING: 5
An okay game. The way the card draft is handled is pretty unique. You have four cards in hand and you can either play a card to the middle of the table as a way to pass, or you can play a card from hand as well as a card from the middle. This way you have to decide to either take whichever cards are available, or possibly help your opponents by playing more cards that they can draft.

The way fields are planted and harvested is also nice. It's basically the wheat-planting mechanic from Agricola/Caverna, but it's a more central mechanic of this game.

I've got a couple of issues with the game. The luck factor of the card draft can be huge. Sometimes you need a certain type of card to come up and it just doesn't. Other times the opposite is true. For example, a player may build two types of vegetables early, say cabbage and turnips, and a regular customer who needs cabbage and turnips comes up in the next card draft. That player now has a steady flow of free money coming in for at least a few rounds. If that doesn't happen, you usually have to work pretty hard to get anywhere because money and resources are really tight. 

Another issues is the way points are handled. At the end of each round you can pay one dollar to move up on the score track, and then pay an amount of money equal to the amount of points to move up more steps. This is a neat idea in theory but near the end of the game it can be obvious who is going to win, especially all throughout the final round or two. Most information is open so you can easily tell if somebody will have enough money to move up enough steps to win.

A few unique ideas, but I would rather play many other games over this one.

Sold.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Loyang was one of the big games getting a lot of buzz in the weeks leading up to Essen, and rightfully so as it's the follow-up release to Agricola and Le Havre (although designed prior to either according to Uwe). However, the game did surprisingly poorly on the GeekBuzz ranking at Essen and again at BGG.CON. Before having had a chance to try Loyang I wondered whether the low ratings were due to response bias (i.e., those predisposed to like Loyang were buying it sight unseen and therefore not playing/rating it, while undecided people were playing and rating it). After having had a chance to try Loyang, I think its low ratings are instead likely due to the disappointment that many fans of the first two installments in the "Harvest Trilogy" felt in seeing how much Loyang diverged from its brethren and how it felt like a step backwards from Uwe's games released over the past couple years. I need to try Loyang again with fewer players, but I didn't feel as if the game worked at all with 4 players. It's likely a two-player game at heart; a dry, procedural, mathematical two-player game (with significant randomness of the two-pack card draw thrown in to majorly disrupt your precise planning), but the designer and/or publisher tried to stretch the game to include up to 4 players. To combat the inevitable downtime and boredom that results from this distortion, the game instructs you to have two players acting simultaneously at any given time, although the pairings will shift from turn to turn. This idea sounds all well and good until you discover that there is an intriguing card draft at the beginning of each round, including some "helper cards" that can disrupt an opponent for your personal gain. Since the four-player game requires the simultaneous-play rules to move at a reasonable pace, you are limited to only playing cards that affect one other member of the opposite pairing. This does not work. You only get to keep two cards per draft, which are generally resources and customer cards to whom you sell your resources for money, with which you buy victory points. A few of the cards are these "helper cards" that can steal resources, customers, and assorted other things from opponents, but sadly it's not opponents, rather opponent. Without knowing how the pairings will work out each turn, you can have no idea whatsoever whether these cards you're drafting will be useful or completely useless. That being said, even with fewer players, I just can't see this measuring up to the competition created by Uwe himself. In Agricola you get to build a farm, try out card combinations, and vie with opponents for limited actions/resources. In Le Havre you get to amass a mountain of resources and buildings giving you a massive playground in which to convert and sell your resources while trying to extort visitation fees from opponents and not take out too many loans in the process. In Loyang you "get to" slowly and painfully creep your way up the score track by pinching every penny as you play through a rather solitaire experience of manipulating your small number of available cards each turn, and ultimately get to end up tied with most of your opponents and seemingly likely decide the game by a tiebreaker. Take your pick.

RATING: 6.5
There is a good game in here somewhere. This is either a solo game for three players or a light three-player game that is over-complicated and somewhat broken. I have rated it as the second of these options because it would get a worse rating (5) as the first option.

I have only played it once, but the problems with it were apparent from that. If played like a solo game it is of course excruciatingly slow. Why not play a proper solo game? If played as a light game, it is just too involved and much of the potential of the game is lost.

In addition to these issues, there are two further problems. One is that the sometimes aggressive player interaction is much less subtle than the solo part of the game and makes the game more clumsy than it was intended to be. The other issue is that I suspect that experienced players would often draw with other experienced players in victory points - it becomes a big thing to make the next point level. Comparing money is too weak a means of choosing a winner in this situation.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Solo plays only. Best solo Euro that exists (edit: perhaps eclipsed by Spirit Island, but they're so different it's not an either/or).

- It doesn't rely on convoluted resource conversion chains like Le Havre and OeL. There's a place for this directness. While there is a great deal of puzzle-solving calculations, the churning is localized & you arrive at decisions (options) rather than scrying a min/max jumble.

- The scoring mechanism is a wonderful (and self-inflicted) constraint on the economy. Loyang gets it's angstiness from it's pain mechanic. As soon as you do put together a sequence of planned turns and cash out, that cash is drastically reduced by buying out VPs and this keeps the game pressurized. At midgame, struggling on whether to take three steps up the trail & be broke with dwindling fields is a tough call. In feel, it relates more to Agricola than the free-wheeling point harvesting of Le Havre and OeL but with a wide open action selection rather than the constraint of worker placement.

- No damned expansions! ;)

- It feels simple to set up and get going. It sits for me in that London and Tinners' Trail rubric of lesser effort than it's larger and more ambitious sibs but doing what it does so well.

- The cards are simple shoves rather than rambunctious combo-litious, and there are some lovely combos but discrete rather than throwing the table. 

- It's somehow calming. It has an almost archetypal feel, the characters on the cards and the notion of operating a vegetable business. I don't know how else to put this. 

- It's gorgeous. Putting all those wooden veggies mixed together into a big bamboo bowl feels like you're reaching into a bowl of Lucky Charms, but out of the box poured only the marshmallows. 

- I've never understood the complaint that a score of 17 + 12 coin beating 17 doesn't feel meaningful. That's a huge spread. Like the richter scale - an 8 earthquake is a hell of a lot bigger than a 7 - and I like how it maps the way tempo has been successfully or less successfully grasped to make a jump rather than an iteration along a straight line.

- It's a chicken soup game. Play this when you're sick sometime. You'll feel better.

RATING: 7

At the Gates of Loyang is a trading game in which you are able to produce goods by planting them and later selling them to customers. You can use the abilities of some helpers to increase your income or production.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
At the Gates of Loyang is a trading game in which you are able to produce goods by planting them and later selling them to customers. You can use the abilities of some helpers to increase your income or production.

Fields, customers, helpers, and miscellaneous objects are represented by cards. Each player receives two of these cards per round distributed by a bidding/drawing mechanism in which you end up with one of the cards you draw and one of the cards of a public offer filled by all players. Additionally, to these cards you always receive one field for free each round.

Placing one good on a field fills the complete field with goods of this type. Each round, one unit per field is harvested. After planting, harvesting, and distributing cards, each player can use as many actions as he wants, only limited by the number of his cards or the number of goods he owns. At the end of his turn, he can invest the earned money on a scoring track, where early money is worth more than late money. The game ends after a certain number of rounds, and the player who is first on the scoring track wins.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Tough game to rate effectively. Probably a few more plays will consolidate my ratings but I have a sneaky suspicion that the extra few plays will bump the ratings down. At the core of it, the game is about building an efficient economic engine to power you through nine rounds of vegetable selling and fulfilling orders. Fulfilling specific orders will net you cold hard cash which could be used to buy victory points, not unlike Princes of Florence, except that victory points cannot be earned any other method. I like the novel scoring system, but unlike others, the card drafting does not work for me. Too many cards on the tableu and too chaotic. The idea behind it is good, just the implementation.

RATING: 9
This is another excellent, albeit underrated, Uwe Rosenberg masterpiece!  It happily accommodates 1-4 players but 2 players is its sweet spot.  Loyang is basically a strategy card game in which you are a farmer/entrepreneur in ancient Loyang, China.  The components are high quality wooden veggie-meeples and small individual "T Squares" which serve as your marketplace and scoreboard.  The bright, colorful artwork is beautiful and appropriately Asian in theme.  Perhaps for the price that this one fetches the coins should have been better made than the cardboard disks that are included.  The scoring mechanism is clever as is the way cards enter the game via a courtyard where players may trade them indirectly in a community pool each round.  Game play consists of careful hand management and timing the purchasing, planting, and harvesting of  vegetables in order to fulfill regular and casual customer demands in timely fashion...all whilst trying to outsmart your competitors with the aid of specially powered helpers.  Efficiency and strategic planning are keys to success in this elegant, unique, and "meaty"  but vegetarian contest.  Expect to make tough choices in Loyang.  Expect to have fun solving market challenges.  Enjoy this game's almost addictive qualities and also its high replayability.  I own all of Uwe's top rated creations and At the Gates of Loyang is one of my very favorites to play.  Treat yourself by giving this one a try!   - Tycoon on BGG, April 2016

RATING: 6
This game would really be good if it just played in the amount of time that it claims on the box.  The turns are so very very long, so there is a lot of downtime, especially with the 3 player rules.

There's little interaction between players, but the interaction that does occur causes AP (analysis paralysis).  After a player finally decides to go with decision A over B, because of the slight possibility of some obscure case that may come up, 80% of the time you find that the situation couldn't have even occurred.

If you have a gameboy or iphone with games with you while you play, then it's not bad.  If watching someone else agonize over decisions is not your cup of tea, though, then stay away from this game.

Unfortunately, I think this game is far shy of living up to the legacies from the other two in the series (Agricola and Le Havre).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
At the Gates of Loyang is a trading game in which you are able to produce goods by planting them and later selling them to customers. You can use the abilities of some helpers to increase your income or production.
\13\\13\
Fields, customers, helpers, and miscellaneous objects are represented by cards. Each player receives two of these cards per round distributed by a bidding/drawing mechanism in which you end up with one of the cards you draw and one of the cards of a public offer filled by all players. Additionally, to these cards you always receive one field for free each round.
\13\\13\
Placing one good on a field fills the complete field with goods of this type. Each round, one unit per field is harvested. After planting, harvesting, and distributing cards, each player can use as many actions as he wants, only limited by the number of his cards or the number of goods he owns. At the end of his turn, he can invest the earned money on a scoring track, where early money is worth more than late money. The game ends after a certain number of rounds, and the player who is first on the scoring track wins.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
At the Gates of Loyang has the reputation of being somewhat of a disappointment. That isn't all that surprising, considering it was released hot on the heels of Agricola and Le Havre, as the third volume of Uwe Rosenberg's Harvest Trilogy. While I have to agree that it is nowhere near as good as those first two titles, I don't really share in the opinion that it is a bad game. 

In fact, I'd say that it is one of the better 2-player games in my collection. Each round of the game features a quick card draft and then a few minutes of agonizing resource management, both of which are features that I enjoy. I also think that the solitaire rules in Loyang are the most interesting of the Trilogy's solo variants, so here again it fills a valuable role in my collection.

The game's four player mode is a bit awkward though, the weird fluctuating trading partner rules are just more trouble than they are worth.But honestly...I doubt that I would even consider playing Loyang if I had four people at the table. There are so many other games in my collection that work better with four, that I'd just rather play one of those.

If I had paid full retail at a FLGS I might be a lot less satisfied with the game, but as I snagged my copy for under 25 bucks I am perfectly happy with it.

On a side note, it's been well documented that while this game was the last released, it is actually the oldest design of the Harvest Trilogy. From a game design standpoint, it's quite interesting to recognize the aspects of Loyang that were inspired by Bohnanza and then in turn to see what parts of Loyang inspired both Agricola and Le Havre. 

Solitaire High Score: 15 points, 14 cash.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a very subtle game...probably too subtle for me. There is player interaction...but aside from the card phase (where decisions may or may not matter hugely to the other players depending on your hand), it's entirely possible to go several turns without directly interacting with your opponent(s). This is an odd feeling in a game that feels a lot like a worker placement game. Probably my biggest nitpick is that the macro strategy seems to be pretty much the same each time: set up fields, then use the engine to get paid. I guess you could say that every game with a VP-type system only has one path to victory if you distill it, but this one seemed to offer fewer choices in how to get there than others (which is ironic, given that mechanically it really does feel like a worker placement game where you have infinite workers and no blocking). In Agricola, for example, you're looking to have a diversified farm by the end of the game, but how are you going to build your engine - vegetables, animals, or can you get a good card combo going? This is not to say that there aren't decisions - there are, and interesting ones at that, which is why I still give it a 7. However, they seem to me to be nuanced choices (I want to say tactical, but I don't think that's entirely accurate) rather than ones that give you freedom to pursue other paths. (An example of something I've tried but I don't think is viable is trying to sacrifice some planting power to support a large, early customer base and buy points while they're cheap.) Hence my statement at the beginning that it may be too subtle for me. Two other, minor negative points: the downtime can be severe at times, and it's possible for the outcome to be a forgone conclusion a few rounds before the game actually ends. Neither of these are huge or significantly worse than other games I've played with these issues. My rating may end up rising with further plays if I can discern more variability in gameplay rather than just better min/maxing, and I would play it again, so we'll see.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Kind of a tough game for me to rate.  It's tempting to compare it to Agricola since they're both by Uwe Rosenberg and about farming.  Even though they're otherwise not that similar, I'm going to do just that.

I really enjoy the puzzly logistics of Loyang a lot, and that aspect is emphasized much more in Loyang than Agricola.  However, there is much less interaction in Loyang than in Agricola, and not as much of the rewarding feeling of "building something cool" (i.e. a farm).

In Agricola, each player takes one action per turn, with 2-5 actions per round, and players are competing for the same actions.  This leads to lots of interaction, but less ability to pull off amazing logistical "combos", which are frequent in Loyang (if you're clever).

In Loyang, players take an arbitrary number of actions all at once (often 10+, though I've never actually counted); only a few of these impact other players.  This leads to a more isolated puzzle-like feel, with less interaction but more flexibility and individual control.  I haven't tried the solitaire game yet, but this seems like the rare case where that may be the best way to play.  The other players probably add more time to the game than interest.  That sounds more damning than I really mean--the game works great with multiple players, but it's biggest strengths should come out just fine in the solo game while also being much quicker (except that there will be nobody to witness your genius).

I actually like that there are many fewer cards in Loyang, and the same ones are used each game.  It's true that this also leads to less variety, but I'm more likely to play this game a few dozen times than several hundred, so I'm fine with that.  It provides a more balanced game and a nicer learning curve, and that's more important to me.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
This game has a unique drafting system, and a unique way to get points. Otherwise it's a nice conversion type of game, but there is one huge problem that sours the whole thing for me. Other than the drafting the game would be multiplayer solitaire, so the designer put in some "interactive" cards. These are some of the lowest form of take that type interaction and while that's a matter of taste (i hate it and i strongly feel it's lazy design) they drag the game down incredibly because otherwise you could do your turns simultaneously and then it would be a one hour game, this way it multiplies with each player. My other problem is even though the game has a nice version of drafting/reverse bidding the game kind of spits in it's own face, by giving you one field each turn semirandomly and the ability to buy two random cards each turn which it seems like you should do a lot. So in a drafting game you get half your cards randomly? Then why draft? I just don't get it. Just imagine if in 7 wonders you did the drafting, then draw some other cards randomly. (This also makes the game even longer because after you carefully planned and drafted, you get two other random cards that if they are any good you need to re-plan a lot of things).

There is a bit of an arc - the middle of the game is the most interesting but the last two turns are mostly determined ahead of time and it feels like what you're doing doesn't matter. 6 Jul 2017 - Ended up trading this away because of the above issues, especially the last few turns where things just peter out. The helper cards often seem a bit useless and I really didn't enjoy the drafting mechanism - I think a simpler "draw x cards and each player selects them one at a time" would work better, especially as it would reduce turn length, which is a major problem in the mid-game. I heard this is good solo, but I didn't ever have the desire to try it. Bumping my rating to a 5.

RATING: 7.5
Juego adquirido en pedido conjunto a Bélgica, por 19,95€.
Recibido el 13/07/2012
Lo he probado en solitario el 11/10/2012, tres meses después, para poder explicarlo en la sesión del viernes del BdC.
Juego de gestión de recursos de 1 a 4 jugadores, en lo que prima básicamente es buscarte tu combo de cartas. 
Los jugadores comercian con verduras tratando de sacarles el máximo beneficio. Posteriormente, en la última fase de cada turno, te puedes gastar el dinero en la denominada "Senda de la prosperidad", que es lo que te da la victoria al final de la partida.

RATING: 8.7
So far this is my favorite game by designer Uwe Rosenberg.  I would say that it is my favorite because it plays so well with two players and is heavier, yet very accessible.  I also love how you have to manage paying for extra points earlier at a cheaper rate or waiting until later in the game but having to pay more. This is one of the few worker placement games were actually feel a part of the theme.  It's like I am managing a market with vegetables. I also like how you can use cards to steal resources from the other player or attack them in some sort of fashion.

Note:  Sleeved with Mayday Premium Euro Card Size Sleeves

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A lot less complicated than its predecessors.  It also requires less time commitment, which is a plus for me.  Unfortunately, the downside to that is it seems to lack the subtlety of Agricola and Le Havre.  There just aren't too many interesting strategy you can do with the limited card selections.  One session is enough to see all there is to offer and I'm afraid that it won't hold up well in multiple plays.

The drafting mechanism is pretty neat.  The partnering, however, feels rather tacked on, since it is only useful in certain scenarios while most of the time it is just another step you have to go through because it doesn't affect your choices what so ever.

I also don't think the penalty for not satisfying an unhappy customer is severe enough, since more often than not paying the two coins is a lot less of a hassle than trying to get the right produces.  Then there is the "strategy" of trading the same item at the market stall just for the sake of using up the card.  Sure, everyone does it, but why create a meaningless loophole just so it can be exploited?

At the Gates of Loyang is a decent game, but also a lot more flawed than Rosenberg's other titles.

RATING: 8
Third in the Uwe Rosenberg 'trilogy' with Agricola and Le Havre.

Rosenberg liked his Agricola planting/harvesting mechanic so much that he decided to expand it into a game of its own.  It stands up well.

AtGoL isn't nearly as intuitive as Gric or Le Havre, but I think it's actually less complex once you get the rules down.  Can be more prone to AP than Gric.  Lots of downtime in 3p.

Love the veggie-ples.

RATING: 10
Trifecta!!  Wow, this is brilliant and agonizing.  I love it!!

[b]4p[/b]: [b][bgcolor=#00cc00] 10 [/bgcolor][/b] The four-player simul-play is a brilliant way to cut the downtime to ⅓ what it would have been with purely sequential play.

[b]3p[/b]: [b][bgcolor=#33cc99] 9 [/bgcolor][/b] The downtime is doubled, but not quite excruciating; however, it does more than double the length of the 2p/4p game.

[b]2p[/b]: [b][bgcolor=#00cc00] 10 [/bgcolor][/b] Simply excellent.

[b]1p[/b]: [b][bgcolor=#00cc00] 10 [/bgcolor][/b] I love it.  It's a meaty, logistical solitaire game that you can play in under an hour.  The action card grid is delightfully torturous, yet permits for some clever strategic play and combo setup - something you typically don't have in any solitaire game!  (Exception: [thing=35443][/thing])  It's wonderful that Uwe's given us a sufficiently different solitaire experience that I actually specifically want to play this solitaire in addition to multiplayer.  That there is a hook of a mechanism that is unique to the solo game is quite cool!
